The Emergence of the Mu'tazila and the Origin of Kalam
Historical origin of kalam with the Mu'tazila and Wasil ibn 'Ata.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a theological dispute within early Islam led to the formation of the Mu'tazila school and the discipline of kalam.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila arose from an internal Islamic debate, not foreign influence.

This lesson traces the historical roots of Islamic theology. We begin with the famous incident of Wasil ibn 'Ata withdrawing from the circle of al-Hasan al-Basri, an event that gave rise to the Mu'tazila and ultimately to the systematic study of kalam. The passage also reveals the Mu'tazila's core commitments of justice and monotheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the historical narrative come through before you interpret.
Notice the cause-and-effect chain from dispute to naming.
2
Identify the key event
The passage describes Wasil ibn 'Ata leaving al-Hasan al-Basri's circle. Find the specific doctrinal point that caused this.
The doctrine is 'al-manzilah bayna al-manzilatayn'.
3
Understand the doctrine of the intermediate state
Wasil argued that a committer of a major sin is neither believer nor disbeliever. Reflect on why this was controversial.
This directly challenges the binary view of faith.
4
Note the Mu'tazila's self-designation
The Mu'tazila called themselves 'ahl al-'adl wa al-tawhid'. See how the passage explains this name by their doctrines of justice and negation of attributes.
This reveals their core theological commitments.
5
Reflect on the significance for kalam
Consider that this internal debate was the spark for systematic theological reasoning. Ask yourself how this story corrects misconceptions about kalam's origins.
The passage emphasizes that kalam emerged from Islamic sources, not external philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبب نشأة علم الكلام ومعظم خلافياته مع الفرق الإسلامية. خصوصا المعتزلة لأنهم أول فرقة أسسوا قواعد الخلاف لما ورد به ظاهر السنة، وجرى عليه جماعة من الصحابة - رضوان الله عليهم أجمعين - في باب العقائد. وذلك أن رئيسهم واصل بن عطاء اعتزل مجلس الحسن البصري - رحمه الله - ليقرر أن مرتكب الكبيرة ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر، ويثبت المنزلة بين المنزلتين. فقال الحسن: قد اعتزل عنا فسموا المعتزلة. وهم سموا أنفسهم أصحاب العدل والتوحيد لقولهم بوجوب ثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي على الله، ونفى الصفات القديمة عنه.
The cause of the emergence of the science of kalam. And most of its controversies are with the Islamic sects, especially the Mu'tazila, because they were the first sect to establish the principles of opposition to what is apparent from the Sunnah and what a group of the Companions – may Allah be pleased with them all – followed in the chapter of beliefs. That is because their leader Wasil ibn 'Ata withdrew from the circle of al-Hasan al-Basri – may Allah have mercy on him – to affirm that the committer of a major sin is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, and to establish the station between the two stations. So al-Hasan said: 'He has withdrawn from us,' so they were called Mu'tazila. And they called themselves 'the people of justice and monotheism' because of their assertion that reward for the obedient and punishment for the disobedient is obligatory upon Allah, and their negation of eternal attributes from Him.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage narrates the historical origin of 'ilm al-kalam as a systematic discipline, focusing on the role of the Mu'tazila. It states that most of the controversies in kalam are with Islamic sects, especially the Mu'tazila, because they were the first to establish frameworks for opposing what was apparent from the Sunnah and the practice of the Companions in beliefs. The specific event is the withdrawal (i'tizal) of Wasil ibn 'Ata from the study circle of al-Hasan al-Basri. Wasil argued that the perpetrator of a major sin (kabirah) is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, but in an intermediate position (al-manzilah bayna al-manzilatayn). Al-Hasan declared that Wasil had withdrawn (i'tazala), giving the sect its name. The Mu'tazila called themselves 'ahl al-'adl wa al-tawhid' (people of justice and monotheism) because they held that reward and punishment are obligatory on God (based on justice) and they denied the eternal attributes (to preserve absolute unity). This origin story highlights how a theological dispute led to the formation of a systematic school of kalam.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
واصل بن عطاء
الحسن البصري
المنزلة بين المنزلتين
مرتكب الكبيرة
أهل العدل والتوحيد
نفي الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think kalam started purely from Greek philosophy or external influences.
Correction: The text shows that kalam emerged from an internal Islamic debate about the status of grave sinners, specifically the dispute between Wasil ibn 'Ata and al-Hasan al-Basri.

Checkpoint
What was the key doctrine that led to Wasil ibn 'Ata's withdrawal and the naming of the Mu'tazila?
A) The attribute of God's speech is created.
B) The perpetrator of a major sin is in an intermediate state.
C) Faith is only affirmation in the heart.
D) Divine justice requires free will.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila originated from an internal Islamic dispute about the status of grave sinners, leading to the first systematic school of kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's justice, do you expect Him to reward your good deeds and punish your sins as a right owed to you, or as His mercy and wisdom? How does that expectation shape your daily acts of worship?
?
The Mu'tazila were willing to break from their teacher over a nuanced theological point. Have you ever clung to an opinion that isolated you from the mainstream community of believers? What might that reveal about your heart's attachment to being 'right' rather than being united?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed to protect God's unity by denying His attributes, yet scripture affirms them. Where in your own understanding of tawhid might you be overzealously removing something that Allah has affirmed about Himself? How can you balance divine transcendence with the descriptions in revelation?

The Origin of the Mu'tazila and Their Role in Kalam
The author explains the origin of kalam with the Mu'tazila and their doctrines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical origin of the Mu'tazila, their foundational beliefs, and how they became the first systematic theological school in Islam.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila were the first to turn theological disagreement into a systematic discipline.

In this passage, the author traces the roots of kalam to the Mu'tazila, who emerged from a split in al-Hasan al-Basri's circle over the status of the grave sinner. Their leader, Wasil ibn 'Ata, formulated the doctrine of 'the station between the two stations,' sparking a new approach to theology. This lesson sets the stage for understanding how the Mu'tazila shaped kalam before the rise of Sunni orthodoxy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the origin of the Mu'tazila
Read the passage carefully to understand the context of the emergence of the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the key event: Wasil's withdrawal
Notice that Wasil ibn 'Ata withdrew from al-Hasan al-Basri's circle over the issue of the grave sinner.
This withdrawal gave the sect its name.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila's self-description
They called themselves 'people of justice and tawhid', which reflects their core theological principles.
They believed God must reward and punish justly and denied eternal attributes.
4
Note their deep engagement with philosophy
The passage mentions they delved deeply into kalam and clung to the tails of philosophers.
This shows their rationalistic approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبب نشأة علم الكلام
ومعظم خلافياته مع الفرق الإسلامية. خصوصا المعتزلة لأنهم أول فرقة أسسوا قواعد الخلاف لما ورد به ظاهر السنة، وجرى عليه جماعة من الصحابة - رضوان الله عليهم أجمعين - في باب العقائد. وذلك أن رئيسهم واصل بن عطاء اعتزل مجلس الحسن البصري - رحمه الله - ليقرر أن مرتكب الكبيرة ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر، ويثبت المنزلة بين المنزلتين. فقال الحسن: قد اعتزل عنا فسموا المعتزلة. وهم سموا أنفسهم أصحاب العدل والتوحيد لقولهم بوجوب ثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي على الله، ونفى الصفات القديمة عنه. ثم إنهم توغلوا في علم الكلام، وتشبثوا بأذيال الفلاسفة في كثير من الأصول، وشاع مذهبهم فيما بين الناس، إلى أن قال الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري لأستاذه أبي علي الجبائي: ما تقول في ثلاثة إخوة مات أحدهم مطيعا، والآخر عاصيا والثالث صغيرا؟ فقال: الأول يثاب بالجنة. والثاني يعاقب بالنار. والثالث لا يثاب ولا يعاقب. قال الأشعري: فإن قال الثالث: يا رب لم أمتني صغيرا، وما أبقيتني إلى أن أكبر فأومن بك وأطيعك فأدخل الجنة؟ (ماذا يقول الرب تعالى؟). فقال: يقول الرب إني كنت أعلم أنك لو كبرت لعصيت فدخلت النار، فكان الأصلح لك أن تموت صغيرا. قال الأشعري: فإن قال الثاني: يا رب لم لم تمتني صغيرا لئلا أعصي فلا أدخل النار؟ فماذا يقول الرب؟ فبهت الجبائي. وترك الأشعري مذهبه، واشتغل هو ومن تبعه بإبطال رأي المعتزلة وإثبات ما وردت به السنة ومضى عليه الجماعة. فسموا أهل السنة والجماعة.
The reason for the emergence of the science of kalam
And most of its disputations are with the Islamic sects, especially the Mu'tazila because they were the first sect to establish the rules of disagreement regarding what was transmitted by the apparent [meaning of] the Sunna and what the group of Companions—may Allah be pleased with them all—followed in the chapter of beliefs. That is because their leader, Wasil ibn 'Ata, withdrew from the circle of al-Hasan al-Basri—may Allah have mercy on him—to establish that the perpetrator of a major sin is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, and to affirm the station between the two stations. So al-Hasan said: 'He has withdrawn from us,' so they were called 'the Mu'tazila.' And they called themselves 'the people of justice and tawhid' because of their statement that it is obligatory upon Allah to reward the obedient and punish the disobedient, and they negated eternal attributes from Him. Then they delved deeply into the science of kalam, and clung to the tails of the philosophers in many principles, and their doctrine spread among the people, until the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said to his teacher Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i: 'What do you say about three brothers: one died obedient, another died disobedient, and the third died as a child?' He replied: 'The first will be rewarded with Paradise, the second will be punished with Hellfire, and the third will neither be rewarded nor punished.' Al-Ash'ari said: 'If the third says: O Lord, why did You cause me to die as a child and not keep me alive until I could grow up, believe in You, and obey You, so that I could enter Paradise? (What would the Lord, exalted, say?)' He replied: 'The Lord says: I knew that if you had grown up, you would have disobeyed and entered Hellfire, so it was better for you to die as a child.' Al-Ash'ari said: 'If the second says: O Lord, why did You not cause me to die as a child so that I would not disobey and enter Hellfire? So what would the Lord say?' Then al-Jubba'i was confounded. And al-Ash'ari left his [the Mu'tazili] doctrine, and he and his followers occupied themselves with refuting the opinion of the Mu'tazila and affirming what the Sunna brought and what the community followed. So they were called 'Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a' (the People of the Sunna and the Community).
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage explains the origin of the science of kalam as a discipline of debate with opposing sects, especially the Mu'tazila. The author notes that the Mu'tazila were the first to systematize disagreement over matters of faith, following their leader Wasil ibn 'Ata who separated from al-Hasan al-Basri's circle over the issue of the grave sinner. Wasil declared that the grave sinner is neither believer nor disbeliever but in an intermediate state, which became known as 'the station between the two stations'. Al-Hasan then said 'he has withdrawn from us', giving them the name 'Mu'tazila' (the withdrawers). They called themselves 'the people of justice and tawhid' because they believed God must reward and punish justly and they denied eternal attributes. They later delved deep into kalam and adopted many philosophical principles. Their influence spread until the famous incident between al-Ash'ari and his teacher al-Jubba'i: al-Ash'ari posed a scenario of three brothers with different fates, and the teacher's answer led to a contradiction that al-Jubba'i could not resolve. This caused al-Ash'ari to abandon Mu'tazilism and dedicate himself to refuting it, establishing the orthodox school of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
واصل بن عطاء
الحسن البصري
المنزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila were purely rationalists disconnected from Islamic sources.
Correction: The passage shows they based their initial disagreement on the apparent meaning of the Sunna and the practice of the Companions.

Checkpoint
What was the immediate cause for the Mu'tazila being named as such?
A) They called themselves 'people of justice'.
B) Al-Hasan al-Basri said 'He has withdrawn from us'.
C) They wrote many books.
D) They established rules of disagreement.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila emerged from a split over the status of the grave sinner and became the first to systematize theological debate in Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted that God must reward the obedient and punish the disobedient. Is there an area of your faith where you find yourself saying, 'God would not do that' based on your own sense of fairness? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
Al-Hasan al-Basri said 'He has withdrawn from us,' marking a split. Have you ever separated from a community over a theological point? Was your motivation sincerity or pride? How can you ensure your theological commitments keep you humble?
?
The Mu'tazila 'clung to the tails of the philosophers' while delving into kalam. Are there any intellectual influences—whether from modern philosophy, science, or culture—that you have allowed to shape your understanding of faith more than the Qur'an and Sunna? What will you do to correct that?

The Three Brothers: Al-Ash'ari's Conversion
The author recounts the famous debate that caused al-Ash'ari's conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the story of the three brothers that led al-Ash'ari to abandon Mu'tazilism and how it established the Sunni position on divine justice.

Core Idea
A logical argument about three brothers exposed a contradiction in Mu'tazili theology.

This powerful anecdote marks a turning point in Islamic theology. Imam al-Ash'ari, a student of the Mu'tazili scholar al-Jubba'i, presented a logical scenario about three brothers with different fates. When his teacher could not answer the objection of one of the brothers, al-Ash'ari realized the weaknesses of Mu'tazili rationalism and embraced the Sunni creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of the three brothers
Read the dialogue between al-Ash'ari and al-Jubba'i carefully.
2
Identify the problem
Notice how the third brother's question leads to a contradiction.
3
Understand the conclusion
The teacher's confusion leads al-Ash'ari to leave Mu'tazilism and join Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبب نشأة علم الكلام
ومعظم خلافياته مع الفرق الإسلامية. خصوصا المعتزلة لأنهم أول فرقة أسسوا قواعد الخلاف لما ورد به ظاهر السنة، وجرى عليه جماعة من الصحابة - رضوان الله عليهم أجمعين - في باب العقائد. وذلك أن رئيسهم واصل بن عطاء اعتزل مجلس الحسن البصري - رحمه الله - ليقرر أن مرتكب الكبيرة ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر، ويثبت المنزلة بين المنزلتين. فقال الحسن: قد اعتزل عنا فسموا المعتزلة. وهم سموا أنفسهم أصحاب العدل والتوحيد لقولهم بوجوب ثواب المطيع وعقاب العاصي على الله، ونفى الصفات القديمة عنه. ثم إنهم توغلوا في علم الكلام، وتشبثوا بأذيال الفلاسفة في كثير من الأصول، وشاع مذهبهم فيما بين الناس، إلى أن قال الشيخ أبو الحسن الأشعري لأستاذه أبي علي الجبائي: ما تقول في ثلاثة إخوة مات أحدهم مطيعا، والآخر عاصيا والثالث صغيرا؟ فقال: الأول يثاب بالجنة. والثاني يعاقب بالنار. والثالث لا يثاب ولا يعاقب. قال الأشعري: فإن قال الثالث: يا رب لم أمتني صغيرا، وما أبقيتني إلى أن أكبر فأومن بك وأطيعك فأدخل الجنة؟ (ماذا يقول الرب تعالى؟). فقال: يقول الرب إني كنت أعلم أنك لو كبرت لعصيت فدخلت النار، فكان الأصلح لك أن تموت صغيرا. قال الأشعري: فإن قال الثاني: يا رب لم لم تمتني صغيرا لئلا أعصي فلا أدخل النار؟ فماذا يقول الرب؟ فبهت الجبائي. وترك الأشعري مذهبه، واشتغل هو ومن تبعه بإبطال رأي المعتزلة وإثبات ما وردت به السنة ومضى عليه الجماعة. فسموا أهل السنة والجماعة.
The reason for the emergence of the science of kalam
And most of its disputations are with the Islamic sects, especially the Mu'tazila because they were the first sect to establish the rules of disagreement regarding what was transmitted by the apparent [meaning of] the Sunna and what the group of Companions—may Allah be pleased with them all—followed in the chapter of beliefs. That is because their leader, Wasil ibn 'Ata, withdrew from the circle of al-Hasan al-Basri—may Allah have mercy on him—to establish that the perpetrator of a major sin is neither a believer nor a disbeliever, and to affirm the station between the two stations. So al-Hasan said: 'He has withdrawn from us,' so they were called 'the Mu'tazila.' And they called themselves 'the people of justice and tawhid' because of their statement that it is obligatory upon Allah to reward the obedient and punish the disobedient, and they negated eternal attributes from Him. Then they delved deeply into the science of kalam, and clung to the tails of the philosophers in many principles, and their doctrine spread among the people, until the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari said to his teacher Abu 'Ali al-Jubba'i: 'What do you say about three brothers: one died obedient, another died disobedient, and the third died as a child?' He replied: 'The first will be rewarded with Paradise, the second will be punished with Hellfire, and the third will neither be rewarded nor punished.' Al-Ash'ari said: 'If the third says: O Lord, why did You cause me to die as a child and not keep me alive until I could grow up, believe in You, and obey You, so that I could enter Paradise? (What would the Lord, exalted, say?)' He replied: 'The Lord says: I knew that if you had grown up, you would have disobeyed and entered Hellfire, so it was better for you to die as a child.' Al-Ash'ari said: 'If the second says: O Lord, why did You not cause me to die as a child so that I would not disobey and enter Hellfire? So what would the Lord say?' Then al-Jubba'i was confounded. And al-Ash'ari left his [the Mu'tazili] doctrine, and he and his followers occupied themselves with refuting the opinion of the Mu'tazila and affirming what the Sunna brought and what the community followed. So they were called 'Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a' (the People of the Sunna and the Community).
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage explains the origin of the science of kalam as a discipline of debate with opposing sects, especially the Mu'tazila. The author notes that the Mu'tazila were the first to systematize disagreement over matters of faith, following their leader Wasil ibn 'Ata who separated from al-Hasan al-Basri's circle over the issue of the grave sinner. Wasil declared that the grave sinner is neither believer nor disbeliever but in an intermediate state, which became known as 'the station between the two stations'. Al-Hasan then said 'he has withdrawn from us', giving them the name 'Mu'tazila' (the withdrawers). They called themselves 'the people of justice and tawhid' because they believed God must reward and punish justly and they denied eternal attributes. They later delved deep into kalam and adopted many philosophical principles. Their influence spread until the famous incident between al-Ash'ari and his teacher al-Jubba'i: al-Ash'ari posed a scenario of three brothers with different fates, and the teacher's answer led to a contradiction that al-Jubba'i could not resolve. This caused al-Ash'ari to abandon Mu'tazilism and dedicate himself to refuting it, establishing the orthodox school of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a.

Key Terms
أبو الحسن الأشعري
أبو علي الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ash'ari abandoned Mu'tazilism due to external pressure or a dream.
Correction: The text clearly states it was a logical argument that confounded his teacher and led to his conversion.

Checkpoint
What was al-Ash'ari's first question in the debate?
A) What about a child who died?
B) What do you say about three brothers?
C) Why does God allow evil?
D) Is reward obligatory?

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's logical question exposed the shortcomings of Mu'tazili rationalism, leading to the formation of Sunni theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a logical contradiction in your understanding of God's justice, do you, like al-Jubba'i, remain confounded, or do you humbly submit to what the Sunna teaches, even if your mind cannot fully comprehend it? What would it take for you to abandon a comfortable but flawed belief for the truth, as al-Ash'ari did?
?
Al-Ash'ari was willing to leave his teacher and his entire school when he saw the truth. Is there a belief you hold because it is familiar or from a respected teacher, even though it contradicts the clear teachings of the Sunna? Will you have the courage to re-examine it, even if it costs you relationships or status?
?
The three brothers argument exposes the limits of human reason in grasping divine wisdom. When your own reasoning leads you to a conclusion about Allah that seems inconsistent, do you trust your intellect or do you submit to revelation? Which do you truly rely on in your daily life? Reflect on a specific situation where you chose one over the other.

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
6 min
Knowledge of Realities and Refutation of the Sophists
Affirms knowledge of realities and refutes Sophist denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological affirmation that objective knowledge of realities is possible and how it refutes the two main Sophist positions.

Core Idea
Realities are objectively knowable, contrary to skeptics and relativists.

This passage confronts a radical challenge to knowledge: the Sophists who deny objective truth. The author affirms that knowledge of realities is realized through conceptualization and affirmation, and then refutes two Sophist sects—one that denies reality outright, and another that makes reality depend on human belief. This is a foundational defense of objective truth in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a claim, an objection, an answer, and a refutation of two groups.
The passage is dense; take time to let the argument unfold.
2
Identify the main claim and objection
The passage begins by stating that knowledge of realities is 'realized' (mutahaqqiq). Then it presents an objection: some say we cannot know all realities. Find how the author answers this.
The answer clarifies that the knowledge refers to the genus of realities, not every single one.
3
Understand the two Sophist groups
The passage names two sects: the 'Inadiyya (obstinate) who deny realities as illusions, and the 'Indiyya (relativists) who say realities are dependent on belief. Distinguish their positions.
The 'Indiyya are labeled as a sect of the Sophists in the footnote.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is it important for Islamic theology to affirm objective reality? How does this passage equip a theologian against skepticism?
This connects to the broader project of establishing a sound foundation for knowledge of God and creation.

والعلم بها أى بالحقائق من تصوراتها والتصديق بها الحقائقَ مِنَ وبأحوالها (متحقق). وقيل : المراد العلم بثبوتها : للقطع بأنه لا علم بجميع الحقائق. والجواب : أن المراد الجنس، ردا على القائلين بأنه لا ثبوت لشيء من الحقائق، ولا علم بثبوت حقيقة ولا بعدم ثبوتها. خلافا للسوفسطائية فإن منهم من ينكر حقائق الأشياء ويزعم أنها أوهام وخيالات باطلة وهم العنادية. ومنهم من ينكر ثبوتها ويزعم أنها تابعة للاعتقادات، حتى إن اعتقدنا الشيء جوهرا فجوهر أو عرضا فعرض أو قديما فقديم أو حادثا فحادث وهم العندية. (۱) فرقة من فرق السوفسطائية.
And knowledge of them (i.e., the realities) – through their conceptualizations (tasawwurat) and affirmations (tasdiq) of the realities and their states – is (mutahaqqiq, realized). And it was said: The intended meaning is knowledge of their establishment, because it is certain that there is no knowledge of all realities. The answer: The intended [knowledge] pertains to the genus (jins), as a refutation of those who say that there is no establishment of any reality, nor knowledge of the establishment of any reality nor of its non-establishment. Contrary to the Sophists (al-Sufistaiyyah): some of them deny the realities of things and claim that they are mere illusions and false fantasies – they are the 'Inadiyya (obstinate ones). And some of them deny their establishment and claim that they are dependent on beliefs, such that if we believe a thing to be substance it becomes substance, or accident, or eternal, or originated – they are the 'Indiyya (relativists). (1) A sect of the Sophists.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage deals with the possibility of knowing the realities. It states that knowledge of the realities is realized through conceptualization (tasawwur) and affirmation (tasdiq) of them and their states. Then it addresses an objection: some say that it is impossible to know all realities, so the statement about knowledge is problematic. The answer clarifies that the intended knowledge is of the genus (jins) – i.e., that there is a category of realities – and is meant to refute those who deny any establishment of realities. This is directed against the Sophists (al-Sufistaiyyah). The Sophists are divided into two groups: the 'Inadiyya, who deny the existence of realities outright, claiming they are illusory; and the 'Indiyya, who deny their fixed establishment, claiming that realities are relative to beliefs (i.e., if we believe something to be a substance, it becomes a substance, etc.). The footnote identifies the second group as a sect of the Sophists. The passage thus defends the objective reality of things against skepticism and relativism.

تصديق

عنادية
عندية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation of the Sophists is merely about linguistic or semantic arguments.
Correction: The passage refutes ontological skepticism and relativism, affirming that realities exist objectively independent of human belief.

Checkpoint
How do the 'Indiyya (relativists) among the Sophists view realities?
A) They deny them entirely.
B) They believe realities are dependent on human beliefs.
C) They accept them as divinely ordained.
D) They claim realities are unknowable.

Key Takeaway
Islamic theology affirms that objective knowledge of realities is possible, and refutes both the denial of reality and its relativization to human belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has your own heart ever entertained the thought that what Allah has revealed is not absolutely true, or that truth might be relative to your circumstances? How does this passage expose that as a form of Sophism, and what will you do to uproot it?
?
In what areas of your life do you live as if reality depends on your own perceptions or desires, rather than submitting to the fixed truths revealed by Allah? Name one specific belief you will test against this lesson today.
?
When you encounter a disagreement about Islamic teachings, do you first assume that your own understanding is correct, or do you submit to the objective realities affirmed by the scholars? This lesson calls you to reject both denial and relativism; where will you begin to hold firmer to the truth?

The Three Types of Sophists (Sufistaiyyah)
Classification of sophists into three types: those denying reality, those making reality dependent on belief, and those doubting knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main positions of the Sophists and why the third position is self-refuting.

Core Idea
Sophists deny objective reality in three ways: rejection, subjectivism, or radical doubt.

In this passage, the author introduces the Sophists (السوفسطائية) as a group that opposes the rational foundations of knowledge. He distinguishes three distinct schools among them: those who deny reality outright, those who make reality dependent on individual belief, and those who doubt all knowledge. This classification helps us recognize and refute various forms of skepticism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three groups
Read the passage and list the three sub-sects of the Sophists mentioned by the author.
Pay attention to the Arabic names: العنادية, العندية, اللا أدرية.
2
Understand each position
For each group, grasp its core claim: denial of reality, belief-dependent reality, or radical doubt.
The second group (العندية) is particularly interesting because it makes reality subjective.
3
Analyze the critique
Focus on the author's note about the third group. Why does he say it leads to circularity and infinite regress?
Consider: if one doubts everything, then one must doubt one's own doubt, leading to an endless chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلافا للسوفسطائية) فإن منهم من ينكر حقائق الأشياء ويزعم أنها أوهام وخيالات باطلة وهم العنادية (فرقة من فرق السوفسطائية). ومنهم من ينكر ثبوتها ويزعم أنها تابعة للاعتقادات، حتى إن اعتقدنا الشيء جوهرا فجوهر أو عرضا فعرض أو قديما فقديم أو حادثا فحادث وهم العندية . ومنهم من ينكر العلم بثبوت شئ ولا ثبوته ويزعم أنه شاك وشاك في أنه شاك وهلم جرا وهم اللا أدرية (وهذا المستحيل تحققه لأنه يؤدى إلى الدور والتسلسل).
Contrary to the Sophists. Among them are those who deny the realities of things and claim that they are false illusions and imaginings; they are the 'Inadiiyya' (a sect of the Sophists). And among them are those who deny their establishment and claim that they are dependent on beliefs, such that if we believe a thing to be a substance, then it is a substance; or an accident, then an accident; or eternal, then eternal; or originated, then originated; they are the 'Indiyya'. And among them are those who deny knowledge of the establishment of anything or its non-establishment, claiming that they are doubters, and doubters that they are doubters, and so on; they are the 'La Adriyya' (and this is impossible to realize because it leads to circularity and infinite regress).
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage classifies the sophists (السوفسطائية) into three groups. (1) العنادية: those who outright deny the reality of things, claiming they are illusions. (2) العندية: those who deny objective reality and claim that things are dependent on our beliefs — what we believe to be a substance is a substance, etc. (3) اللا أدرية: those who deny knowledge of whether anything is real or not, falling into infinite doubt. The author adds a critical note: this third position is impossible because it leads to circular reasoning and infinite regress.

Key Terms

العنادية
العندية
اللا أدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sophists hold the same view, that nothing is real.
Correction: The author distinguishes three distinct positions: outright denial, subjectivism, and radical skepticism.

Checkpoint
Which group of sophists claims that things are dependent on our beliefs?
A) العنادية
B) العندية
C) اللا أدرية
D) All of them

Key Takeaway
The Sophists are not a single group; they include deniers, subjectivists, and skeptics, and the last position is self-defeating due to circularity and infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you acted like the 'Inadiyya, refusing to accept a truth because it conflicted with your desires? How can you cultivate humility to submit to reality even when it is uncomfortable?
?
The 'Indiyya claim reality depends on belief. In what area of your life have you insisted something is true just because you believed it, ignoring clear evidence? What step will you take this week to anchor your beliefs in objective truth rather than wishful thinking?
?
The La Adriyya fall into endless doubt. Have you ever found yourself paralyzed by uncertainty, doubting even your own faith? How does knowing that this position leads to logical absurdity spur you to seek certainty from Allah's revelation rather than from your own fluctuating mind?

Refuting Skepticism: The Two Proofs
Taftazani defines the La Adriyya and gives two proofs against them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two proofs Taftazani uses against absolute skeptics and their logical force.

Core Idea
To deny all knowledge is self-refuting.

Taftazani begins by identifying the most extreme skeptics—the La Adriyya—who deny all certainty. He then presents two powerful arguments: a verification based on necessary knowledge, and a compulsion that even denial affirms reality. This lesson unpacks these proofs and their significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation to grasp the author's argument flow.
Notice the structure: definition, then two proofs introduced by 'lana tahqiqan wa ilzaman'.
2
Understand the skeptics' position
Identify what the La Adriyya claim: they deny knowledge of existence and non-existence, and even doubt their own doubt.
3
Analyze the verification proof
The author states we are certain by necessity of some things via direct perception (al-‘iyan) and via proof (al-bayan). Ask: what are examples?
This proof relies on necessary knowledge.
4
Analyze the compulsion proof
The author argues that even to deny all realities is itself to affirm a reality, because denial is a judgment. So absolute denial is impossible.
Note the caveat: this only works against the obstinate.
5
Summarize the two proofs
Write in your own words how verification and compulsion work. Why are they effective?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من ينكر العلم بثبوت شئ ولا ثبوته ويزعم أنه شاك وشاك في أنه شاك وهلم جرا وهم اللا أدرية . لنا تحقيقا : أنا نجزم بالضرورة بثبوت بعض الأشياء بالعيان، وبعضها بالبيان. وإلزاما : أنه إن لم يتحقق نفى الأشياء، فقد ثبتت، وإن تحقق فالنفى حقيقة من الحقائق، لكونه نوعا من الحكم، فقد ثبت شئ من الحقائق، فلم يصح نفيها على الإطلاق. ولا يخفى أنه إنما يتم على العنادية . + :قالوا: الضروريات منها حسيات والحس قد يغلط كثيرا، كالأحول يرى الواحد اثنين والصفراوي يجد الحلو مرا . ومنها بديهيات. قد يقع فيها اختلافات . وتعرض شبه يفتقر في حلها إلى أنظار دقيقة. والنظريات فرع الضروريات. ففسادها .فسادها ولهذا كثر فيها اختلاف العقلاء. قلنا : غلط الحس فى البعض، لأسباب جزئية، لا ينافي الجزم بالبعض بانتفاء أسباب الغلط والاختلاف فى البديهي لعدم الإلف أو الخفاء في التصور، لا ينافي البداهة. وكثرة الاختلافات لفساد الانظار لا تنافى حقيقة بعض النظريات . والحق أنه لا طريق إلى المناظرة معهم، خصوصا اللا أدرية لأنهم لا يعترفون بمعلوم، ليثبت به مجهول بل الطريق تعذيبهم بالنار ليعترفوا أو يحترقوا.
And among them are those who deny knowledge of the existence of anything or its non-existence, and claim that he is a skeptic, and skeptical about being skeptical, and so on. They are the al-La Adriyya. For us, a verification: we are certain by necessity of the existence of some things by sight, and some by proof. And a compulsion: if he has not realized the negation of things, then they are established; and if he has realized it, then negation is one of the realities, because it is a type of judgment, so something has been established from realities, so it is not valid to deny them absolutely. And it is not hidden that this only works against the obstinate. They said: Necessary things include sensory things, and the senses often err, like the one-eyed person sees one as two, and the jaundiced person finds sweet as bitter. And among them are intuitive things; differences occur in them, and doubts arise that require precise considerations to resolve. And theoretical things are branches of necessary things; their corruption is their corruption, and for this reason differences among rational people abound. We said: The error of the senses in some cases, due to particular causes, does not negate certainty in some cases by the absence of causes of error. And difference in the intuitive due to lack of familiarity or subtlety in the concept does not negate self-evidence. And the abundance of differences due to corruption of considerations does not negate the reality of some theoretical things. And the truth is that there is no way to debate with them, especially the al-La Adriyya, because they do not acknowledge any known thing by which an unknown could be established. Rather, the way is to punish them with fire so that they either acknowledge or burn.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the absolute skeptics (al-La Adriyya), who deny all knowledge. The author presents two proofs: (1) a verification (tahqiqan) based on necessary knowledge – we are certain of some things by direct perception (al-‘iyan) and others by proof (al-bayan); (2) a compulsion (ilzaman) that even to deny all realities is to affirm a reality, because denial itself is a type of judgment. The author notes that this argument only works against the obstinate. Then the sophists object that sensory perception errs (e.g., one-eyed person seeing one as two, jaundiced person tasting sweet as bitter) and that even intuitive truths have differences. The author responds that such errors are due to particular causes and do not undermine all sensory knowledge, and that differences in intuitive matters arise from lack of familiarity or subtlety, not from lack of self-evidence. Finally, the author states that debate is futile with such people; the only way is punishment by fire, as they acknowledge no premise to start from.

Key Terms
اللا أدرية
العلم
الضرورة
العيان
البيان
الإلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two proofs the author gives against the absolute skeptics?
A) A proof from divine revelation and a proof from consensus
B) A proof from verification (tahqiq) and a proof from compulsion (ilzam)
C) A proof from the senses and a proof from reason
D) A proof from authority and a proof from logic

Key Takeaway
The absolute skeptic's position is self-defeating because even denying knowledge requires acknowledging a judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used intellectual doubt as an excuse to avoid submitting to a clear truth from Allah? How does Taftazani's argument that denial itself is a judgment expose the inconsistency in your heart? What truth are you still resisting today?
?
The author says the argument only works against the obstinate. Are you obstinate in rejecting truths that are plain to sight and clear in proof? What would it cost you to admit that you already know them?
?
If you applied the verification argument to your own life, what certainties from Allah have you been living as if they were optional? Will you now act on what you know by necessity?

Responding to Skeptical Objections and the Ultimate Deterrent
Taftazani addresses skeptical objections and concludes debate is futile.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani answers the skeptics' objections about sensory error and differences in intuition, and his final ruling on debating them.

Core Idea
Skeptical doubts about senses and intuition do not undermine all knowledge, but debate is useless.

Having presented two proofs, Taftazani now addresses the skeptics' rebuttal: senses can be mistaken and even intuitive truths are disputed. His response is detailed, showing that such objections do not negate certainty when proper conditions are met. Finally, he concludes that the only effective course is not debate but punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the skeptics' objection starting from 'qalu' (they said). Notice they divide necessary knowledge into sensory and intuitive, and point to errors and disagreements.
Examples: one-eyed person sees one as two; jaundiced person tastes sweet as bitter.
2
Understand the response to sensory error
Taftazani replies that errors occur due to particular causes and do not undermine certainty where those causes are absent.
3
Understand the response to intuitive differences
Differences in intuitive matters arise from lack of familiarity or subtlety in the concept, not from lack of self-evidence.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
Taftazani states that debate is futile because the La Adriyya accept no premise; the only way is punishment by fire.
This is a harsh but logical conclusion given their refusal to engage.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider whether Taftazani's responses fully address the skeptical challenge. Why might punishment be the only option?

Conclusion on the Philosophers' Division of Eternity and Temporality
Concludes a previous argument about the philosophers' division of qidam and huduth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the philosophers' division of eternity and temporality into essential and temporal is a view that the author rejects as violating many principles.

Core Idea
The philosophers' division of qidam and huduth is rejected for undermining foundational principles.

In this brief concluding passage, the author wraps up a previous discussion by identifying the view being criticized as that of the philosophers, who divide both eternity and temporality into essential and temporal categories. He emphasizes that this view contradicts many established principles and promises a more detailed refutation later. This serves as a transition to the next topic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the gist. Notice how the author is concluding a prior argument.
2
Identify the view being described
The passage states the philosophers' position: that both qidam (eternity) and huduth (temporality) are divided into essential and temporal types. Understand that this is not the author's own view.
The author is reporting a view he rejects.
3
Note the author's evaluation
The author says 'fīhi rafḍun li-kathīrin min al-qawā'id' (in it is a rejection of many principles). This indicates the author's disapproval.
This is a key phrase showing the author's stance.
4
Understand the promise of further verification
The author says 'wa sayatī li-hādhā ziyādat taḥqīq' (and there will come for this further verification). This signals that a more detailed discussion will follow.
This is a transitional marker.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو قول بما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة من انقسام كل من القدم والحدوث إلى الذاتي والزماني. وفيه رفض لكثير من القواعد . وسيأتي لهذا زيادة تحقيق.
So it is a statement adhering to what the philosophers held regarding the division of each of eternity and temporality into the essential and the temporal. In it is a rejection of many principles. And there will come for this further verification.

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion to a previous discussion. The author notes that this position rejects many established principles, and promises a more detailed verification later. This serves as a transition to the next topic.

?
The author warns that the philosophers' view 'rejects many principles.' Are there any Islamic principles you have unconsciously compromised because you adopted a modern philosophical or cultural assumption without scrutiny? What will you do today to verify that assumption against revelation?
?
The passage promises 'further verification.' How often do you accept sophisticated-sounding ideas about faith without seeking deeper verification from traditional sources? Name one core belief you will re-examine this week to ensure it aligns with the principles of Ahl al-Sunna.

Four Positions on Divine Attributes
Author enumerates four theological positions on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four major theological positions on divine attributes as outlined by the author, along with the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
The difficulty of the attributes divided theologians into four distinct camps.

This lesson presents a concise yet powerful summary of the major disagreements among Islamic theological schools regarding the nature of God's attributes. The author acknowledges the inherent difficulty of this topic, which led different groups to adopt distinct positions. We will examine each position and understand its core claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the author uses a single sentence to list four positions.
2
Identify the four groups
List the four groups mentioned: Mu'tazila, Philosophers, Karrāmiyya, and Ash'ariyya. Note that the first two share one position.
The author groups Mu'tazila and philosophers together.
3
Understand each position
For each group, identify what they are negating: attributes (Mu'tazila/Philosophers), pre-eternity (Karrāmiyya), or otherness/identity (Ash'ariyya).
The Ash'ari position is a middle ground between affirmation and negation.
4
Reflect on the difficulty
Consider why the author says 'due to the difficulty of this position.' How does this difficulty explain the diversity of opinions?
This connects to the broader theological challenge of reconciling God's essence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولصعوبة هذا المقام، ذهبت المعتزلة والفلاسفة إلى نفى الصفات. والكرامية إلى نفى قدمها والأشاعرة إلى نفى غيريتها وعينيتها.
And due to the difficulty of this position, the Mu'tazila and the philosophers went to negation of the attributes. And the Karrāmiyya to negation of their pre-eternity. And the Ash'ariyya to negation of their otherness and identity [with the essence].
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the major Islamic theological schools regarding the divine attributes. The author notes that the difficulty of the topic (المقام) led different groups to various positions. The Mu'tazila and the philosophers (الفلاسفة) denied the attributes altogether (نفى الصفات), meaning they believed that God has no attributes distinct from His essence. The Karrāmiyya (الكرامية) denied the pre-eternity (قدم) of the attributes, holding that the attributes are created in time. The Ash'ariyya (الأشاعرة) took a middle position: they affirmed the attributes but denied that they are either other (غيرية) than the essence or identical (عينية) to it. This sets up the objection and discussion that follow.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الفلاسفة
الكرامية
الأشاعرة
نفى الصفات
نفى قدمها
نفى غيريتها وعينيتها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari position implies attributes are completely identical to the essence or completely other.
Correction: The Ash'ariyya affirm the attributes but deny that they are either entirely other (غيرية) or entirely identical (عينية) to the essence. This is a nuanced middle ground.

Checkpoint
Which group denied the pre-eternity of the divine attributes?
A) Mu'tazila
B) Philosophers
C) Karrāmiyya
D) Ash'ariyya

Key Takeaway
The difficulty of the attributes led to four distinct positions, among which the Ash'ari view is a balanced middle path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a difficult theological question about God's nature, do you tend to reject complexity like the Mu'tazila, or do you avoid it entirely? How does this passage challenge you to embrace the balanced middle path of the Ash'ariyya?
?
The Karrāmiyya denied the pre-eternity of God's attributes—have you ever considered that God's attributes might be created? What does that reveal about your understanding of God's perfection?
?
The Ash'ariyya maintained a delicate balance between affirming attributes and avoiding extremes. In what area of your faith are you tempted to take an extreme view rather than accepting a difficult middle position? What is holding you back from that balance?

Response to the Hanabila Objection on the Quran's Inimitability
The author deflects the Hanabila objection by pointing to the created nature of the Quran's organization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'arites deflect the Hanabila objection by distinguishing between the temporal organization of the Quran and its eternal meaning.

Core Idea
The Quran's inimitability lies in the eternal meaning, not the created letters.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection raised by the Hanabila who argue that the miraculous inimitability (i'jaz) of the Quran would have to apply to created expressions. The author clarifies that this argument only refutes the Hanabila position, not the Ash'arite one, because they distinguish between the temporal arrangement of words and the ancient meaning that is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Note that it begins with 'إلى غير ذلك' indicating it is part of a larger discussion.
The passage is a direct response to an objection.
2
Identify the two parties
The author contrasts 'الحنابلة' (Hanabila) with 'علينا' (us, the Ash'arites). Understand that the objection is from the Hanabila, and the author is replying on behalf of the Ash'arites.
The Hanabila are a theological group who held that the Quran is created in its letters.
3
Analyze the key distinction
Notice the two key concepts: 'حدوث التنظيم' (temporality of organization) and 'المعنى القديم' (the ancient meaning). The author affirms that the arrangement is created but the meaning is eternal. This distinction allows them to deflect the objection.
The discussion is only about the eternal meaning, not the created expressions.
4
Understand the defensive move
The author claims the objection only works against the Hanabila themselves. Why? Because if the Quran's inimitability required a created object, then the Ash'arite view of an eternal meaning avoids that problem. The Hanabila view would face the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى غير ذلك فإنما يقوم حجة على الحنابلة لا علينا. لأنا قائلون بحدوث التنظيم وإنما الكلام في المعنى القديم.
And so forth, it only stands as a proof against the Hanabila, not against us. For we affirm the temporality of the organization [of the Quran], and the discussion is only about the ancient meaning.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection. The objector (the Hanabila) argues that if the Quran is only the ancient meaning, then the miraculous inimitability (i'jaz) cannot be attributed to the revealed letters. The author replies that the proof stands against the Hanabila, not against the Ash'arites, because they (the Ash'arites) hold that the organization (nazm) of the Quran is temporally originated (hadith). The real point of contention is the ancient meaning (al-ma'na al-qadim), which is eternal and attributed to Allah. The author thus deflects the objection by clarifying that the discussion is about the uncreated meaning, not the created expressions.

Key Terms
الحنابلة
الحدوث
التنظيم
المعنى القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'arites deny the Quran's inimitability because they say the Quran is created.
Correction: Ash'arites affirm that the Quran's inimitability lies in its eternal meaning (al-ma'na al-qadim), not in the temporal organization of words.

Checkpoint

A) Against the Ash'arites
B) Against the Hanabila
C) Against the Mu'tazila
D) Against all groups

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arite view locates the Quran's inimitability in the eternal meaning, not the temporal arrangement of words.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran today, are you consciously connecting with the eternal meaning behind the words, or are you merely engaging with the temporal arrangement? How can you shift your focus to the ancient meaning that Allah intended?

?
The Ash'arites distinguish between created organization and uncreated meaning. In your own life, do you often mistake the form (how you pray, how you memorize) for the substance (the connection with Allah)? What can you do today to prioritize the eternal meaning over the temporal form?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Divine Speech
The author presents the Mu'tazila view and refutes it with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila interpretation of Allah as a speaker by creating speech is theologically inadequate according to Ash'ari reasoning.

Core Idea
A true attribute of speech must inhere in the speaker, not just be created.

This passage confronts a theological challenge from the Mu'tazila who, unable to deny that Allah speaks, redefined 'speaker' as one who creates speech elsewhere. The author refutes this by a simple but powerful analogy, showing that such a definition collapses crucial distinctions between the Creator and His creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the key terms: المتكلم, إيجاد, محال.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila could not deny that Allah is a speaker, so they reinterpreted it. Find their exact definition in the text.
They say He creates sounds and letters in a locus.
3
Understand the refutation analogy
The author uses the analogy of a 'mover' (المتحرك). Explain how this analogy refutes the Mu'tazila definition.
The key is that the attribute must subsist in the subject.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
If the Mu'tazila view were true, then Allah would be described by accidents He creates, which is impossible. Consider why this is a problem.
Accidents are created things; Allah is not a locus for created attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة لما لم يمكنهم إنكار كونه تعالى متكلما ذهبوا إلى أنه متكلم بمعنى إيجاد الأصوات والحروف فى محالها، أو إيجاد أشكال الكتابة في اللوح المحفوظ، وإن لم يقرأ على اختلاف بينهم. وأنت خبير بأن المتحرك : من قامت به الحركة، لا من أوجدها وإلا لصح اتصاف البارى بالأعراض المخلوقة له - تعالى عن ذلك علوا كبيرا.
And the Mu'tazila, since they could not deny that He—exalted is He—is a speaker, went to [the position] that He is a speaker in the sense of creating sounds and letters in their loci, or creating the forms of writing in the Preserved Tablet, even if not recited, according to their differences. And you are well aware that the 'mover' is the one in whom motion subsists, not the one who brings it into existence—otherwise it would be valid to describe the Creator with the accidents created by Him—exalted is He above that, greatly.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position on divine speech. First, the author states the Mu'tazila view: they could not deny that Allah is a speaker (mutakallim), so they interpreted it as meaning that He creates sounds and letters in some locus (such as the air or the Preserved Tablet). Then the author refutes this by analogy: a 'mover' (mutaharrik) is one in whom motion subsists (qāma bihi al-ḥaraka), not one who creates motion elsewhere. If that were the case, God would be described by the accidents He creates, which is impossible. Thus, Allah cannot be described as 'speaker' merely by creating speech in another locus; rather, speech must subsist in His essence.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
متكلما
إيجاد الأصوات والحروف
محالها
المتحرك
البارى
الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that creating speech is sufficient for being described as a speaker.
Correction: The refutation shows that a true attribute must inhere in the subject; otherwise, the Creator would be described by accidental, created qualities.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the Mu'tazila definition of 'speaker'?
A) Because the Mu'tazila deny that Allah speaks at all
B) Because being a speaker requires speech to subsist in the subject, not merely be created
C) Because sounds and letters are eternal
D) Because the Mu'tazila agree with the Ash'arites

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila redefinition of divine speech is invalid because attributes must inhere in the subject, not merely be created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of Allah's speech—the Qur'an—as something created or separate from His essence? How does knowing that His speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in Him change the way you recite or listen to the Qur'an? Will you, starting today, recite with the conviction that you are uttering the very speech of Allah, not a creation?
?
If a true attribute must inhere in the subject, what does that tell you about the reality of Allah's names and attributes? Which attribute do you most need to internalize as a real, personal quality of Allah rather than a mere title? Commit to reflecting on one attribute each day this week.
?
The Mu'tazila tried to protect Allah's transcendence but ended up diminishing His speech. Have you ever, in trying to protect Allah's majesty, unconsciously reduced an aspect of His perfection? What will you do to ensure your understanding of Allah is not a product of human reasoning alone but rooted in revelation?

Early Theologians Avoided Calling the Servant 'Creator'
Historical note on the avoidance of the term 'creator' for human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why early Mutakallimūn avoided the term 'Creator' for human actions and how al-Jubbā'ī later justified its use.

Core Idea
Early theologians chose cautious language to preserve Allah's exclusive attribute of creation.

Even within the same theological school, terminology can shift. This short passage reveals a historical debate among the Mu'tazila about whether humans can be called 'creators' of their own acts. The early ones chose safer words; al-Jubbā'ī argued that if the meaning is the same, the word is permissible. Let's see how the passage frames this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the contrast between 'the early ones' and 'al-Jubbā'ī and his followers'.
The passage is only two sentences, so pay close attention to each term.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify what the early ones did and what al-Jubbā'ī did. Why did the early ones avoid 'khāliq'? What did they use instead? What argument did al-Jubbā'ī make?
The core issue is whether the meaning of 'mūjid' and 'khāliq' is identical.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: does the passage approve or disapprove of al-Jubbā'ī's move? The word 'dared' (تجاسروا) may imply criticism. This sets up a larger discussion about divine attributes.
This passage is historical, not doctrinal – it describes what happened, not what is correct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد كانت الأوائل منهم يتحاشون عن إطلاق لفظ الخالق على العبد، ويكتفون بلفظ الموجد ،والمخترع ونحو ذلك . وحين رأى الجبائي وأتباعه أن معنى الكل واحد وهو المخرج من العدم إلى الوجود، تجاسروا على إطلاق لفظ الخالق .
The early ones among them used to avoid applying the word 'Creator' (al-Khāliq) to the servant, and they sufficed with the word 'originator' (al-Mūjid) and 'inventor' (al-Mukhtari‘) and the like. When al-Jubbā’ī and his followers saw that the meaning of all is one — namely bringing something from non-existence into existence — they dared to apply the word 'Creator'.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This is a historical observation about the terminology used by early theologians (the Mu'tazila possibly). The early ones avoided calling the human agent 'creator' (khāliq) and instead used terms like 'originator' (mūjid) and 'inventor' (mukhtari‘). Later, al-Jubbā’ī and his followers argued that the meaning of all these terms is the same — bringing something from non-existence into existence — so they felt justified in using the term 'creator' for human actions. This sets up the terminological dispute.

Key Terms

الموجد
المخترع
الجبائي

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because they rejected the need for divine creation altogether.
D) Because they wanted to avoid anthropomorphism.

Key Takeaway
Early Mu'tazila avoided calling humans 'creators', but al-Jubbā'ī later argued it was permissible because the core meaning of the terms is the same.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Al-Jubbā'ī justified using 'Creator' because he saw the meaning as the same, but the early ones avoided it out of deeper caution. How will you choose the more reverent path even when others justify leniency?

The Mu'tazila and the Charge of Shirk
Objection and answer regarding Mu'tazila and shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing creation to humans does not necessarily constitute shirk, and how the Mu'tazila avoid polytheism by distinguishing types of shirk.

Core Idea
Shirk is about divinity, not action—Mu'tazila do not make humans divine.

A strong objection is raised: if Mu'tazila claim humans create their own actions, are they not polytheists? The response carefully defines shirk in terms of divinity, showing that human creativity, being dependent on God, does not impugn divine uniqueness. This lesson also notes an extreme counter-view from some later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice the objection, the response, and the additional remark.
The passage uses a dialectical style: 'It cannot be said... For we say...'.
2
Analyze the definition of shirk
The response defines shirk as affirming a partner in divinity either (a) in necessary existence (as the Magi do) or (b) in deserving worship (as idolaters do). Understand these two categories.
The Mu'tazila do not attribute either of these to humans.
3
Consider the extreme view
Note that some Transoxiana scholars claimed Mu'tazila are worse than Magi. Reflect on why they said that and whether it is a valid conclusion from the definition given.
This shows the danger of exaggeration in theological disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : فالقائل بكون العبد خالقا لأفعاله، يكون من المشركين دون الموحدين. لأنا نقول : الاشتراك هو إثبات الشريك في الألوهية بمعنى وجوب الوجود، كما للمجوس، أو بمعنى استحقاق العبادة، كما لعبدة الأصنام. والمعتزلة لا يثبتون ذلك، بل لا يجعلون خالقية العبد كخالقية الله تعالى، لافتقاره إلى الأسباب والآلات التي هي بخلق الله ،تعالى إلا أن مشايخ ما وراء النهر، قد بالغوا في تضليلهم في هذه المسألة، حتى قالوا : إن المجوس أسعد حالا منهم، حيث لم يثبتوا إلا شريكا ،واحدا والمعتزلة أثبتوا شركاء لا تحصى.
It cannot be said: The one who says that the servant is a creator of his actions is among the polytheists, not the monotheists. For we say: Associating partners (shirk) is affirming a partner in divinity (al-ulūhiyya) in the sense of necessary existence, as with the Magi, or in the sense of deserving worship, as with the idolaters. And the Mu'tazila do not affirm that; rather, they do not make the creativity of the servant equal to the creativity of Allah, Exalted, due to its dependence on causes and instruments that are by the creation of Allah, Exalted. However, the shaykhs of Transoxiana have gone to extremes in declaring them misguided in this matter, so much so that they said: 'The Magi are in a better state than them, for they affirm only one partner, while the Mu'tazila affirm countless partners.'
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The potential objection is that if one says the human is a creator of his actions, he falls into shirk (polytheism) rather than tawhid. The response distinguishes the type of shirk: shirk means affirming a partner in divinity either in terms of necessary existence (wujūb al-wujūd) or in terms of deserving worship (istiḥqāq al-‘ibāda). The Mu'tazila do not attribute either of these to the human; they distinguish human creativity from divine creativity because human actions depend on causes and instruments created by God. Thus, it is not shirk. The passage then notes that some Hanafi/Māturīdī scholars from Transoxiana went so far as to say the Mu'tazila are worse than the Magi, because the Magi only affirm one partner, whereas the Mu'tazila affirm countless partners (since each human is considered a creator of his own actions).

Key Terms
شرك
ألوهية
وجوب الوجود
استحقاق العبادة
مجبوس
عبدة الأصنام
خالقية
مشايخ ما وراء النهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any attribution of creation to a creature is shirk.
Correction: Shirk is specifically about divinity (necessary existence or worthiness of worship), not about creative acts as such.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why is the Mu'tazila belief not considered shirk?
A) Because they deny that humans create anything.
B) Because they do not affirm that humans have necessary existence or deserve worship.
C) Because they limit human creation to non-material things.
D) Because they believe human creation is identical to divine creation.

Key Takeaway
Mu'tazila are not mushrikun because they do not ascribe necessary existence or worship-worthiness to humans, only a dependent form of creativity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your own efforts and actions are entirely independent of Allah? Reflect on a moment when you attributed success solely to your own ability. How does this lesson's definition of shirk as ascribing necessary existence or worship to others apply to your heart's subtle tendencies? What can you change today to redirect reliance back to Allah alone?
?
This passage warns against the extremism of declaring others disbelievers over theological disagreements. Have you ever judged someone as being outside the fold of Islam due to a difference in opinion? What does this lesson teach you about the importance of precise definitions before such judgment? Will you commit to seeking understanding before accusation?
?
The Mu'tazila were accused of shirk despite not believing in partners in divinity. Consider: could you be accusing others of shirk or kufr without understanding their actual beliefs? How can you ensure your judgments are based on sound definitions and not prejudice? What step will you take to avoid such injustice?

Defending Human Agency from the Charge of Shirk
Objection and response on the Mu'tazilite claim of human creatorship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the Mu'tazilite position on human action from the charge of shirk by defining shirk precisely and noting the Mu'tazilites' distinction between human and divine creatorship.

Core Idea
Shirk is not human agency but equating that agency with God's independence.

This passage anticipates a common objection: if the servant creates his actions, doesn't that make him a partner with God? The author responds by clarifying what shirk truly means — affirming a partner in divinity — and shows that the Mu'tazilites do not do that. He also reports an extreme view from Transoxiana scholars that ironically makes the situation worse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and response
Read the passage and identify the anticipated objection. Then find the author's response. Notice how he defines shirk before refuting the objection.
The objection is that the Mu'tazilite view implies shirk.
2
Understand the definition of shirk
The author defines shirk as affirming a partner in divinity, either in necessity of existence (Magians) or in deserving worship (idolaters). Note that the Mu'tazilites do neither.
Shirk here is about divine attributes, not just any creative act.
3
Reflect on the extreme view
The author mentions that some Transoxiana scholars exaggerated, calling the Mu'tazilites worse than Magians. Ponder why this is considered an overreaction.
The author is reporting, not endorsing, this extreme view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذهول عن هذه النكتة قد يتوهم أن الاستدلال بالآية موقوف على كون ( ما ) مصدرية. كقوله تعالى: ﴿ اللَّهُ خَالِقُ كُلِّ شيء ﴾ [الرعد: ١٦] أي ممكن، بدلالة العقل وفعل العبد شي ممكن وكقوله تعالى : ﴿ أَفَمَن يَخْلُقُ كَمَن لا يخلق ﴾ [النحل : ۱۷] في مقام دح بالخالقية وكونها مناطا لاستحقاق العبادة . [التمد؟] لا يقال : فالقائل بكون العبد خالقا لأفعاله، يكون من المشركين دون الموحدين. لأنا نقول : الاشتراك هو إثبات الشريك في الألوهية بمعنى وجوب الوجود، كما للمجوس، أو بمعنى استحقاق العبادة، كما لعبدة الأصنام. والمعتزلة لا يثبتون ذلك، بل لا يجعلون خالقية العبد كخالقية الله تعالى، لافتقاره إلى الأسباب والآلات التي هي بخلق الله تعالى إلا أن مشايخ ما وراء النهر، قد بالغوا في تضليلهم في هذه المسألة، حتى قالوا : إن المجوس أسعد حالا منهم، حيث لم يثبتوا إلا شريكا واحدا والمعتزلة أثبتوا شركاء لا تحصى.
And inadvertence about this subtlety may cause one to think that using the verse as evidence depends on 'ma' being masdariyya. Like His saying, Exalted: 'Allah is the Creator of all things' (13:16) i.e., possible things, by indication of the intellect, and the action of the servant is a possible thing; and like His saying, Exalted: 'Is He who creates like one who does not create?' (16:17) in the context of rebuttal by creatorship and that it is the basis of deserving worship. [؟] It is not said: The one who says that the servant is the creator of his actions is among the polytheists, not the monotheists. For we say: Association (al-ishrak) is affirming a partner in divinity (al-uluhiyya) in the sense of necessity of existence, as with the Magians, or in the sense of deserving worship, as with the idol worshippers. And the Mu'tazila do not affirm that, rather they do not make the creatorship of the servant like the creatorship of Allah, Exalted, due to its need for causes and instruments which are by the creation of Allah, Exalted. But the scholars of Transoxiana have exaggerated in declaring them astray in this issue, until they said: The Magians are more fortunate than them, since they affirmed only one partner, while the Mu'tazila affirmed incalculable partners.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer section, preceded by an introductory clarification about a subtle point in interpreting a Quranic verse. The author first warns that one might mistakenly think that the evidence from the verse 'Allah is the Creator of all things' depends on the particle 'ma' being interpreted as a masdariyya (infinitive particle). He clarifies that the verse applies to all possible things, and the servant's action is a possible thing, so it is included. Then he cites another verse 'Is He who creates like one who does not create?' to argue that creatorship is the basis for worship. After this, a word [التمد؟] appears which is unclear, possibly a transition. Then an objector says: 'It should not be said that the one who claims the servant creates his actions is among the polytheists?' Actually the wording 'لا يقال' means an anticipated objection: 'One might say: The one who says the servant is the creator of his actions is among the polytheists, not the monotheists.' The author responds: 'For we say:' and then defines shirk (al-ishrak) as affirming a partner in divinity (al-uluhiyya) either in the sense of necessary existence (as the Magians believe) or in the sense of deserving worship (as idol worshippers believe). The Mu'tazila do not affirm either; they do not equate the servant's creatorship with God's, since the servant's creative act depends on causes and instruments that are themselves created by God. The author then notes that the scholars of Transoxiana have been excessive in declaring the Mu'tazila as astray, even saying that the Magians are better off because they only affirm one partner (an eternal evil principle) while the Mu'tazila affirm countless partners (every human action). This is a contextual remark, not necessarily the author's own view, but a report of the extreme position taken by some scholars.

Key Terms
إشراك
ألوهية
وجوب الوجود
خالقية
المجوس
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling the servant a creator of his actions is automatically shirk because it attributes creation to other than God.
Correction: Shirk is specifically equating that creatorship with God's in terms of divinity (necessity of existence or deserving worship), which the Mu'tazilites do not do.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the definition of shirk (al-ishrak)?
A) Affirming a partner in divinity in the sense of necessity of existence or deserving worship.
B) Saying the servant creates his actions.
C) Denying divine attributes.
D) Claiming that God creates evil.

Key Takeaway
Shirk is defined as affirming a partner in divinity, not any creative act, and the Mu'tazilites maintain a distinction between human and divine creatorship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged someone's belief as shirk without fully understanding what they actually affirm? How does this lesson's precise definition of shirk challenge you to be more careful in your judgments?
?
The author notes that some scholars exaggerated in declaring the Mu'tazila astray. Examine your own heart: do you have a tendency to label those who disagree with you as deviant? What step will you take to seek understanding before condemning?
?
This passage shows that even a belief in human creatorship is not automatically shirk if it is not associated with divine independence. In your worship, do you guard against subtle forms of associating partners with God, such as relying on created means as if they are independent? How can you purify your reliance on God alone?
