The Magian's Retort: God's Will Over All
Anecdote illustrating argument for God's universal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the anecdote of Amr ibn Ubayd and the Magian exposes the logical inconsistency in any theology that limits God's all-encompassing will.

Core Idea
The Magian's retort shows that limiting God's will logically empowers lesser beings.

This anecdote is a classic illustration used by Ash'ari theologians to defend the doctrine of God's universal will. It recounts a dialogue between a Mu'tazili leader and a Magian (Zoroastrian). The Magian's clever response exposes the flaw in any theology that restricts God's will while granting effective power to other agents.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three speaking parts: the narrator (Amr), the Magian, and Amr's reply.
The anecdote is short but packed with logical reasoning.
2
Identify the three positions
Identify what each speaker claims: the Magian says God has not willed his Islam; Amr says God wills but devils prevent; the Magian retorts that he will side with the stronger partner (the devils).
The Magian's second statement is the punchline.
3
Analyze the logical trap
Understand why the Magian's retort is effective: if devils can overcome God's will, they are stronger, which is absurd. Thus, one must accept that God's will is always effective.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Connect to the broader issue
Relate this anecdote to the theological debate on divine will and human responsibility. The Ash'ari position holds that God's will is all-encompassing, while the Mu'tazilites limit it to preserve human free will.
This anecdote is a defense of the Ash'ari view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكى عن عمرو بن عبيد (1) أنه قال: ما الزمنى أحد مثل ما الزمني مجوسي كان معي في السفينة. فقلت له: لم لا تسلم فقال: لأن الله لم يرد إسلامى: فإذا أراد الله إسلامي أسلمت فقلت للمجوسى: إن الله تعالى يريد إسلامك، ولكن الشياطين لا يتركونك. فقال المجوسى: فأنا أكون مع الشريك الأغلب (1) أحد أئمة المعتزلة - هداهم الله.
It is related from Amr ibn Ubayd (1) that he said: No one has confounded me as much as a Magian who was with me in the ship. I said to him: Why do you not accept Islam? He said: Because Allah has not willed my Islam; when Allah wills my Islam, I will become Muslim. I said to the Magian: Allah, exalted, wills your Islam, but the devils do not let you. The Magian said: Then I will be with the stronger partner. (1) One of the leaders of the Mu'tazila - may Allah guide them.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is an anecdote illustrating an earlier point about divine will. Amr ibn Ubayd, a Mu'tazili leader, recounts how a Magian confounded him. The Magian says he does not convert because God has not willed his Islam. Amr responds that God wills his Islam but the devils prevent him. The Magian then makes a clever retort: he will side with the stronger partner—implying that if God wills Islam but devils prevent it, then the devils are stronger, which is absurd. The anecdote supports the Ash'ari position that God's will is all-encompassing and not limited by any other power.

Key Terms
إرادة
مجوسي
شريك أغلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this anecdote supports the Mu'tazili position that God's will is limited by other agents.
Correction: In fact, the anecdote is used to refute that view by showing that if devils can override God's will, they would be stronger, which is absurd. Thus, it supports the Ash'ari view of God's all-encompassing will.

Checkpoint
What is the Magian's argument against Amr ibn Ubayd's claim that God wills his Islam but devils prevent it?
A) That God is not powerful enough
B) That he will side with the stronger partner (the devils)
C) That devils do not exist
D) That God's will is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The anecdote demonstrates that any theology denying God's universal will leads to absurd consequences, such as a created being overpowering the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that nothing happens in your life except by Allah's will, or do you sometimes feel that events slip outside His control? How does your reaction to disappointment reveal your true conviction?
?
When you see evil or injustice prevail, do you doubt Allah's power or wisdom? Consider the Magian's argument: if you attribute independent power to any being besides Allah, you are effectively accepting a partner. Where in your heart have you done that?
?
The Magian used Allah's will as an excuse for his disbelief. Are there areas of your life where you use qadr as a passive excuse rather than a call to action? Examine your response to trials: does your belief in divine will lead to obedience or negligence?

Refuting the Charge of Shirking Divine Power
Answers objection that kasb theory implies partnership with God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb avoids shirk by attributing the same act to God and man through two different aspects.

Core Idea
Attribution by different aspects is not shirk.

When the Ash'aris say that both God and the servant are involved in the human action, critics charge that this is shirk—associating partners with God. But is every dual attribution shirk? The answer is no, as this passage clarifies by distinguishing true partnership from attribution by different aspects. Let's examine the definition and the key analogy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening objection: the opponent claims that affirming kasb is like affirming partnership with God, as the Mu'tazila do. What is their reasoning?
The Mu'tazila held that humans create their own actions, which implies a partnership in creation.
2
Grasp the definition of shirk
Notice the precise definition: shirk is when two agents come together on one thing, each being alone with his own share. This is not the same as attributing an act to two agents by different aspects.
The definition uses the example of partners in a village – each partner owns his portion separately.
3
Apply the analogy of land
The land analogy shows that one thing (land) can be attributed to God as Creator and to humans as disposers without shirk. Similarly, the act of the servant is attributed to God by creation and to the servant by acquisition.
The key is the phrase 'by two different aspects' (bi-jihatayn mukhtalifatayn).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فقد أثبتم ما نسبتم إلى المعتزلة من إثبات الشركة. قلنا : الشركة أن يجتمع اثنان على شيء واحد، وينفرد كل منهما بما هو له دون الآخر، كشركاء القرية والمحلة . وكما إذا جعل العبد خالقا لأفعاله ، والصانع خالقا لسائر الأعراض والأجسام، بخلاف ما إذا أضيف أمر إلى شيئين بجهتين مختلفتين كارض تكون ملكا الله تعالى بجهة التخليق، وللعباد بجهة ثبوت التصرف، وكفعل العبد ينسب إلى الله تعالى بجهة الخلق، وإلى العبد بجهة الكسب .
If it is said: 'You have affirmed what you attribute to the Mu'tazila of affirming partnership (shirk).' We say: Partnership is that two come together upon one thing, and each one is alone with what belongs to him without the other, like the partners of a village or a neighborhood. And similarly if the servant is made the creator of his own acts, and the Maker is made the creator of all other accidents and bodies, contrary to when a matter is attributed to two things by two different aspects, like land that belongs to God Almighty by way of creation (takhliq) and belongs to the servants by way of established disposal (thubut al-tasarruf), and like the act of the servant is attributed to God Almighty by way of creation (khalq) and to the servant by way of acquisition (kasb).
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This is an objection-answer passage. The objector claims that the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb leads to shirk (associating partners with God) because it attributes human acts to both God and man. The answer refutes this by defining shirk: it is when two agents jointly produce a single effect, each contributing his part independently. In kasb, however, the action is attributed to God by way of creation (khalq) and to man by way of acquisition (kasb) — two different aspects. The analogy of land ownership illustrates: land belongs to God as Creator and to humans as disposers, without shirk. Thus, attributing an act to two sources by different aspects is not shirk.

Key Terms
الشركة
الجهة
التخليق
التصرف
الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any attribution of an action to two agents is shirk.
Correction: Shirk requires two agents jointly producing one effect, each contributing independently. Attribution by different aspects (e.g., creation vs. acquisition) is not shirk.

Checkpoint
How does the passage define shirk (partnership) in the context of divine acts?
A. Attributing an action to two agents by different aspects.
B. Two agents coming together on one thing, each being alone with his own share.
C. Attributing creation to the servant.
D. Denying that God creates all actions.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari doctrine of kasb does not entail shirk because the act is attributed to God by way of creation and to man by way of acquisition—two different aspects, not two independent contributions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your daily actions, do you truly believe that Allah creates them and you only acquire them? If so, how does that change your sense of humility and gratitude right now? If not, what does that reveal about your heart's reliance on your own power?
?
The land analogy says God owns by creation, you own by disposal. What possessions or abilities in your life do you act as if they are entirely yours? Will you consciously acknowledge Allah's ownership of them today by using them in obedience?
?
If every act you do is created by Allah and acquired by you, then neither pride nor despair have a place. Are there areas where you feel pride in your achievements or despair over your failures? How can the truth of this passage free you from both?

First Mu'tazila Proof: The Agreed-Upon Term
First Mu'tazila proof using the consensus method and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument of taking the agreed-upon term (fasiq) and leaving the disputed ones, and why it is rejected as an innovation.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila invented a new position by rejecting the Salaf's consensus.

The Mu'tazila sought to define the grave sinner (fasiq) based on points of agreement among early sects. They claimed that since all agreed he is a fasiq, the additional labels of believer, disbeliever, or hypocrite should be dropped. In this lesson, we examine this reasoning and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the Mu'tazila present their reasoning.
Pay attention to the words 'fasiq', 'mu'min', 'kafir', 'munafiq'.
2
Identify the logical principle
The Mu'tazila use the principle of taking what is agreed upon and leaving the disputed. Identify this in the text.
3
Understand the refutation
The answer calls this an 'innovation' (hadath) that contradicts the Salaf's consensus on rejecting an intermediate station. Grasp why this is considered invalid.
4
Connect to Ahl al-Sunna position
Recognize that Ahl al-Sunna maintain the grave sinner is still a believer, while the Mu'tazila place him in an intermediate state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجت المعتزلة بوجهين الأول : أن الأمة بعد اتفاقهم على أن مرتكب الكبيرة فاسق واختلفوا فى أنه مؤمن - وهو مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة - أو كافر؟ - وهو قول الخوارج - أو منافق - وهو قول الحسن البصرى - فأخذنا المتفق عليه، وتركنا المختلف فيه . وقلنا : هو فاسق وليس بمؤمن ولا كافر ولا منافق . والجواب أن هذا إحداث للقول المخالف لما أجمع عليه السلف من عدم المنزلة بين المنزلتين، فيكون باطلا . والثاني: أنه ليس بمؤمن، لقوله تعالى : (أَفَمَن كَانَ مُؤمِنًا كَمَن كَانَ فَاسقا ﴾ [السجدة: ۱۸] جعل المؤمن مقابلا للفاسق. وقوله عليه السلام لا يزني الزاني حين يزني وهو مؤمن وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : «لا إيمان لمن لا أمانة له . ولا كافر لما تواتر من أن الأمة كانوا لا يقتلونه ولا يجرون عليه أحكام المرتدين، ويدفنونه في مقابر المسلمين. والجواب : أن المراد بالفاسق في الآية هو الكافر، فإن الكفر من أعظم الفسوق والحديث وارد على سبيل التغليظ، والمبالغة في الزجر عن المعاصى، بدليل الآيات والأحاديث الدالة على أن الفاسق مؤمن حتى قال عليه السلام لأبي ذر لما بالغ فى السؤال : ( وإن زنى وإن سرق على رغم أنف أبي ذر ) .
The Mu'tazila argued with two points. First: After they agreed that the committer of a major sin is a fasiq, they differed about whether he is a believer—which is the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a—or a disbeliever?—which is the statement of the Khawarij—or a hypocrite?—which is the statement of al-Hasan al-Basri. So we took the agreed-upon and left the disputed. And we said: He is a fasiq, not a believer, not a disbeliever, and not a hypocrite. The answer: That this is an innovation of a statement contradicting what the Salaf unanimously agreed upon—the negation of the intermediate station between the two stations—so it is false. Second: That he is not a believer, because of His saying: 'Then is he who was a believer like he who was a fasiq?' [al-Sajda 32:18]—He made the believer opposite the fasiq. And his saying, peace be upon him: 'The adulterer does not commit adultery while he is a believer.' And his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'There is no faith for the one who has no trustworthiness.' And he is not a disbeliever, because of what is widely transmitted that the community did not kill him nor apply the rulings of apostates to him, and they bury him in the cemeteries of the Muslims. The answer: That what is meant by 'fasiq' in the verse is the disbeliever, for disbelief is the greatest of fasiq. And the hadith are conveyed by way of exaggeration and stressing the deterrence from sins, as evidence from the verses and hadiths indicating that the fasiq is a believer, until he said, peace be upon him, to Abu Dharr when he went too far in asking: 'Even if he fornicates and even if he steals, despite the nose of Abu Dharr.'
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents two Mu'tazila proofs and their refutations. First proof: The ummah agrees the grave sinner is a fasiq (immoral), but differed about the further label: believer (Ahl al-Sunna), disbeliever (Khawarij), or hypocrite (Hasan al-Basri). The Mu'tazila claim we should take only the agreed term—fasiq—and drop the disputed ones, concluding he is fasiq, not believer, not disbeliever, not hypocrite. The answer: This is an innovation (hadath) contradicting the Salaf who unanimously rejected an intermediate station (manzila bayna al-manzilatayn), so it is false. Second proof: They cite the verse making believer opposite fasiq, and hadiths like 'No adulterer commits adultery while a believer' and 'No faith without trustworthiness', and note the community does not kill or apply apostasy laws to the sinner, yet buries him in Muslim cemeteries—so he is not a disbeliever either. They conclude he is neither believer nor disbeliever. The answer: 'Fasiq' in the verse means disbeliever, since disbelief is the worst fisq. The hadiths are stern warnings (tahghliz) to deter, as proven by many texts affirming the fasiq is a believer, including the hadith that even if he fornicates and steals, faith remains (reported to Abu Dharr).

Key Terms
فاسق
مؤمن
كافر
منافق
منزلة بين منزلتين
إحداث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taking the agreed-upon and leaving the disputed is a sound logical method.
Correction: The Salaf's consensus on rejecting an intermediate station shows this method is an innovation, not a valid inference.

Checkpoint
What is the logical principle used in the first Mu'tazila proof?
A) The majority opinion
B) Taking the agreed-upon and leaving the disputed
C) The apparent meaning of texts
D) Consensus of the Salaf

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's reliance on agreed-upon terms to deny faith to the grave sinner is rejected as an innovation contrary to Salaf's consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall into a major sin, do you ever doubt that you are still a believer? How does the Salaf's refusal to create an intermediate category actually protect your faith and give you a clear path to repentance?
?
Have you ever been tempted to adopt a 'middle ground' Islamic position to avoid conflict or to appear more reasonable? How does the Mu'tazila's well-intentioned but flawed method warn you about the danger of compromising core beliefs for the sake of consensus?
?
If someone told you that your repeated sins have reduced your iman to nothing, would you accept that? What does the Salaf's view say about you—both your responsibility to repent and your permanent identity as a believer, even in your weakest moments?

Second Mu'tazila Proof: Scriptural Evidence
Second Mu'tazila proof using Quran and Hadith and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila scriptural arguments from Quran and Hadith that seem to separate faith from sin, and their refutation showing these are warnings for disbelievers or rhetorical exaggerations.

Core Idea
Scriptural proofs against the sinner's faith are actually about disbelievers or are stern warnings.

The Mu'tazila also cite the Quran and Hadith to argue that a grave sinner cannot be a believer. They point to a verse contrasting believer and fasiq, and hadiths like 'no adulterer commits adultery while a believer'. In this lesson, we examine these texts and their refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic and translation of the second argument. Identify the three pieces of evidence: a verse and two hadiths.
2
Note the community practice argument
The Mu'tazila also note that the community does not treat the fasiq as a disbeliever (no execution, buried in Muslim cemeteries).
3
Understand the refutation
The answer reinterprets 'fasiq' in the verse as 'disbeliever', and the hadiths as exaggerations for deterrence.
4
Connect to the broader doctrine
The refutation appeals to other texts that affirm the fasiq remains a believer, like the hadith to Abu Dharr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجت المعتزلة بوجهين الأول : أن الأمة بعد اتفاقهم على أن مرتكب الكبيرة فاسق واختلفوا فى أنه مؤمن - وهو مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة - أو كافر؟ - وهو قول الخوارج - أو منافق - وهو قول الحسن البصرى - فأخذنا المتفق عليه، وتركنا المختلف فيه . وقلنا : هو فاسق وليس بمؤمن ولا كافر ولا منافق . والجواب أن هذا إحداث للقول المخالف لما أجمع عليه السلف من عدم المنزلة بين المنزلتين، فيكون باطلا . والثاني: أنه ليس بمؤمن، لقوله تعالى : (أَفَمَن كَانَ مُؤمِنًا كَمَن كَانَ فَاسقا ﴾ [السجدة: ۱۸] جعل المؤمن مقابلا للفاسق. وقوله عليه السلام لا يزني الزاني حين يزني وهو مؤمن وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : «لا إيمان لمن لا أمانة له . ولا كافر لما تواتر من أن الأمة كانوا لا يقتلونه ولا يجرون عليه أحكام المرتدين، ويدفنونه في مقابر المسلمين. والجواب : أن المراد بالفاسق في الآية هو الكافر، فإن الكفر من أعظم الفسوق والحديث وارد على سبيل التغليظ، والمبالغة في الزجر عن المعاصى، بدليل الآيات والأحاديث الدالة على أن الفاسق مؤمن حتى قال عليه السلام لأبي ذر لما بالغ فى السؤال : ( وإن زنى وإن سرق على رغم أنف أبي ذر ) .
The Mu'tazila argued with two points. First: After they agreed that the committer of a major sin is a fasiq, they differed about whether he is a believer—which is the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a—or a disbeliever?—which is the statement of the Khawarij—or a hypocrite?—which is the statement of al-Hasan al-Basri. So we took the agreed-upon and left the disputed. And we said: He is a fasiq, not a believer, not a disbeliever, and not a hypocrite. The answer: That this is an innovation of a statement contradicting what the Salaf unanimously agreed upon—the negation of the intermediate station between the two stations—so it is false. Second: That he is not a believer, because of His saying: 'Then is he who was a believer like he who was a fasiq?' [al-Sajda 32:18]—He made the believer opposite the fasiq. And his saying, peace be upon him: 'The adulterer does not commit adultery while he is a believer.' And his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'There is no faith for the one who has no trustworthiness.' And he is not a disbeliever, because of what is widely transmitted that the community did not kill him nor apply the rulings of apostates to him, and they bury him in the cemeteries of the Muslims. The answer: That what is meant by 'fasiq' in the verse is the disbeliever, for disbelief is the greatest of fasiq. And the hadith are conveyed by way of exaggeration and stressing the deterrence from sins, as evidence from the verses and hadiths indicating that the fasiq is a believer, until he said, peace be upon him, to Abu Dharr when he went too far in asking: 'Even if he fornicates and even if he steals, despite the nose of Abu Dharr.'
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents two Mu'tazila proofs and their refutations. First proof: The ummah agrees the grave sinner is a fasiq (immoral), but differed about the further label: believer (Ahl al-Sunna), disbeliever (Khawarij), or hypocrite (Hasan al-Basri). The Mu'tazila claim we should take only the agreed term—fasiq—and drop the disputed ones, concluding he is fasiq, not believer, not disbeliever, not hypocrite. The answer: This is an innovation (hadath) contradicting the Salaf who unanimously rejected an intermediate station (manzila bayna al-manzilatayn), so it is false. Second proof: They cite the verse making believer opposite fasiq, and hadiths like 'No adulterer commits adultery while a believer' and 'No faith without trustworthiness', and note the community does not kill or apply apostasy laws to the sinner, yet buries him in Muslim cemeteries—so he is not a disbeliever either. They conclude he is neither believer nor disbeliever. The answer: 'Fasiq' in the verse means disbeliever, since disbelief is the worst fisq. The hadiths are stern warnings (tahghliz) to deter, as proven by many texts affirming the fasiq is a believer, including the hadith that even if he fornicates and steals, faith remains (reported to Abu Dharr).

مؤمن
كافر
تغليظ
مبالغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'fasiq' always refers to any grave sinner, and the hadiths literally deny faith to sinners.
Correction: In the context of the verse, 'fasiq' means disbeliever; the hadiths are rhetorical warnings.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation interpret the word 'fasiq' in Quran 32:18?
A) It means any grave sinner
B) It means a hypocrite
C) It means a disbeliever
D) It means an innovator

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's scriptural proofs are refuted by reinterpreting 'fasiq' as disbeliever and the hadiths as exaggerated warnings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall into a sin, do you ever doubt that you are still a believer? How does this lesson's clarification that a fasiq remains a believer change the way you view your own spiritual state? Will you now turn to repentance with more hope or with more fear?
?
The Prophet said, 'There is no faith for the one who has no trustworthiness,' and it is explained as a hyperbole. Is there an area in your life where you have been dishonest, and you comfort yourself by saying, 'I'm still a believer'? How will you take this warning seriously today without using it as an excuse to sin?
?
Have you ever looked down on a fellow Muslim because of their sins, thinking their faith is weak? The Prophet told Abu Dharr, 'Even if he fornicates and steals, despite the nose of Abu Dharr.' How does this challenge your attitude? Will you now treat sinners with more mercy and less judgment, knowing that faith can coexist with sin?

The Khawarij Arguments and Their Refutation
The author presents and refutes Khawarij proof texts for takfir of the sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the apparent texts cited by the Khawarij do not prove that a grave sinner becomes a disbeliever, and how definitive texts and consensus override them.

Core Idea
Not every verse calling someone a 'kafir' means they leave Islam — context and consensus matter.

The Khawarij were known for their extreme position that major sins expel a person from faith. They cited several Quranic verses and a hadith to support their claim. In this passage, the author presents their proofs and then gives a concise refutation based on the principle that apparent texts are overridden by definitive texts and the consensus of the Muslim community.

Guided Path
1
Read the Khawarij proofs
Identify the verses and hadith the Khawarij used. Notice they rely on the apparent (zahir) meaning.
The verses include 5:44, 24:55, 20:48, 92:15-16, 16:27.
2
Understand the author's answer
The author says these texts are 'matruka al-zahir' (abandoned in their literal sense) because there are definitive texts and consensus that the grave sinner is not a disbeliever.
3
See the role of consensus
The author emphasizes that the Khawarij have deviated from the consensus, so their opinions are not considered.
The word 'Khawarij' literally means 'those who go out' — from the community.
4
Reflect on the principle
Think about why apparent texts must be interpreted in light of other texts. This is a key principle in usul al-fiqh.
This refutation protects the unity of the Muslim community.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجت الخوارج بالنصوص الظاهرة في أن الفاسق كافر، كقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَنْ لَمْ يَحْكُمْ بِمَا أَنْزَلَ اللَّهُ فَأُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْكَافِرُونَ ﴾ [المائدة: 44] وقوله تعالى: ( وَمَنْ كَفَرَ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ فَأُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْفَاسِقُونَ ) [النور: 55] وكقوله عليه السلام : ( مَنْ تَرَكَ الصَّلَاةَ مُتَعَمِّدًا فَقَدْ كَفَرَ ) وفي أن العذاب مختص بالكافر، كقوله تعالى: ﴿ أَنَّ الْعَذَابَ عَلَى مَنْ كَذَّبَ وَتَوَلَّى ﴾ [طه: 48] وقوله تعالى: ( وَلَا يَصْلَاهَا إِلَّا الْأَشْقَى * الَّذِي كَذَّبَ وَتَوَلَّى ) [الليل: 15-16] وقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ الْخِزْيَ الْيَوْمَ وَالسُّوءَ عَلَى الْكَافِرِينَ ﴾ [النحل: 27] إلى غير ذلك . والجواب : أنها متروكة الظاهر، للنصوص القاطعة، على أن مرتكب الكبيرة ليس بكافر، والإجماع المنعقد على ذلك - على ما مر والخوارج خوارج عما انعقد عليه الإجماع، فلا اعتداد بهم.
And the Khawarij argued with the apparent texts that the fasiq is a disbeliever, such as His saying: 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed, then those are the disbelievers' [al-Ma'ida 5:44], and His saying: 'And whoever disbelieves after that, then those are the defiantly disobedient' [al-Nur 24:55], and his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever leaves prayer intentionally has disbelieved.' And that punishment is specific to the disbeliever, such as His saying: 'Indeed, the punishment is upon whoever denies and turns away' [Ta-Ha 20:48], and His saying: 'And none will burn in it except the most wretched, who has denied and turned away' [al-Layl 92:15-16], and His saying: 'Indeed, disgrace this day and evil are upon the disbelievers' [al-Nahl 16:27], and other [similar texts]. The answer: That they are abandoned in their apparent meaning due to the definitive texts that the committer of a major sin is not a disbeliever, and the consensus established upon that—as has passed—and the Khawarij have deviated from what consensus was established upon, so no heed is paid to them.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the Khawarij proofs and their refutation. The Khawarij cite various apparent (zahir) texts to claim the fasiq is a kafir: the verse about judging by other than Allah's law (them being disbelievers), another verse equating disbelief with fisq, the hadith that leaving prayer intentionally is kufr, and verses stating that punishment is for the disbeliever (the one who denies and turns away). The answer: These texts are abandoned in their apparent meaning (matruka al-zahir) because they are overridden by definitive texts (nusus qati'a) that affirm the grave sinner is not a disbeliever, and by the consensus of the ummah on that. Since the Khawarij have deviated from this consensus (they 'kharij' from it), they are not considered authoritative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a Quranic verse or hadith calls someone a 'kafir' for a sin, that person is certainly a disbeliever and outside the fold of Islam.
Correction: The apparent meaning of such texts is overridden by definitive texts and consensus, which affirm that the grave sinner remains a believer.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why are the Khawarij arguments invalid?
A) Because the verses they cite are not authentic

Key Takeaway
Apparent texts that seem to label a sinner as a disbeliever are overridden by definitive texts and the consensus of the Muslim community.

?

?
If you were to fall into a major sin, would you still believe you are a Muslim, or would despair drive you to doubt your faith? The consensus of Ahl al-Sunnah gives you hope, but also demands that you turn back to Allah. Are you living in a way that honors that mercy?

Refutation of Khawarij on Takfir of Major Sinners
Refutes Khawarij arguments that major sinners are disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni scholars refute the Khawarij position that major sinners become disbelievers, using definitive texts and consensus.

Core Idea
Major sins do not expel one from faith; Khawarij misinterpretation contradicts definitive texts and consensus.

The Khawarij were an early sect that declared major sinners disbelievers, justifying rebellion. Sunni scholars refuted this by showing that the verses and hadiths they cited either refer to disbelief itself or are meant as severe warnings, not literal excommunication. This passage presents the Sunni argument and the response to Khawarij proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for overall meaning. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: Sunni practice, an implied objection, Khawarij proofs, and the Sunni answer.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the Sunni stance on the fasiq
The passage starts by stating the Sunni practice regarding a fasiq (grave sinner) who dies: they do not execute him, do not treat him as an apostate, and bury him in Muslim cemeteries. This shows he is still considered a Muslim.
This is the practical implication of not deeming the fasiq a kafir.
3
Examine the first objection and answer
The author anticipates that some might use the term 'fasiq' in the Quran to claim that a sinner is a disbeliever. The answer clarifies that in the relevant verse, 'fasiq' means the disbeliever, since disbelief is the greatest sin. Hadiths that harshly condemn sin are meant as deterrence, not takfir.
The hadith of Abu Dharr is cited as proof that even adulterers and thieves remain believers.
4
Analyze the Khawarij proofs and the reply
The Khawarij cite verses and hadiths that appear to equate grave sin with disbelief. The author responds that these texts are set aside from their apparent meaning because there are definitive texts (nass qati') and consensus that a major sinner is not a disbeliever.
The Khawarij are dismissed as deviants because they contradict consensus.
5
Reflect on the role of consensus
Note the argument that consensus (ijma') is decisive: the Ummah has agreed that major sins do not remove faith. Khawarij are blamed for breaking away from this consensus.
This shows the importance of scholarly consensus in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقتلونه ولا يجرون عليه أحكام المرتدين، ويدفنونه في مقابر المسلمين. والجواب : أن المراد بالفاسق في الآية هو الكافر، فإن الكفر من أعظم الفسوق والحديث وارد على سبيل التغليظ، والمبالغة في الزجر عن المعاصى، بدليل الآيات والأحاديث الدالة على أن الفاسق مؤمن حتى قال عليه السلام لأبي ذر لما بالغ فى السؤال : ( وإن زنى وإن سرق على رغم أنف أبي ذر ) . واحتجت الخوارج بالنصوص الظاهرة في أن الفاسق كافر، كقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن لَّمْ يَحكُم بِمَا أَنزَلَ اللَّهِ فَأُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْكَافِرُونَ ﴾ [المائدة: 44] وقوله تعالى : ( ومن كفر بعدَ ذَلِكَ فَأُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْفَاسِقُونَ ﴾ [النور: 55] وكقوله عليه السلام : ( من ترك الصلاة متعمدا، فقد كفر ) وفي أن العذاب مختص بالكافر، كقوله تعالى: ﴿ أَنَّ الْعَذَابَ عَلَى مَن كَذَّبَ وتولى ﴾ [طه: 48] وقوله تعالى: ﴿ ولا يَصْلاهَا إِلا الأَشْقَى * الَّذِي كَذَّبَ وتولَّى ﴾ [الليل: 15-16] وقوله تعالى : ﴿ إِنَّ الْخِزْيَ الْيَوْمَ وَالسُّوءَ عَلَى الْكَافِرِينَ ﴾ [النحل: 27] إلى غير ذلك . والجواب : أنها متروكة الظاهر، للنصوص القاطعة، على أن مرتكب الكبيرة ليس بكافر، والإجماع المنعقد على ذلك - على ما مر - والخوارج خوارج عما انعقد عليه الإجماع، فلا اعتداد بهم.
They do not kill him nor apply the rulings of apostates to him, and they bury him in the cemeteries of the Muslims. The answer: that the intended meaning of 'fasiq' in the verse is the disbeliever, for disbelief is among the greatest of sins, and the hadith is understood as a warning and exaggeration in deterrence from sins, as evidenced by the verses and hadiths that indicate that the fasiq is a believer, such that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Abu Dharr when he insisted in asking: 'Even if he commits adultery and steals, despite the nose of Abu Dharr.' And the Khawarij argued with apparent texts that the fasiq is a disbeliever, such as the Almighty's saying: 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed – then it is those who are the disbelievers' [al-Ma'idah 5:44] and His saying: 'And whoever disbelieves after that – then it is those who are the defiantly disobedient' [an-Nur 24:55] and his saying (peace be upon him): 'Whoever intentionally abandons prayer has committed disbelief.' And that punishment is specific to the disbeliever, such as the Almighty's saying: 'that the punishment is upon whoever denies and turns away' [Taha 20:48] and His saying: 'And none shall enter it except the wretched one who denies and turns away' [al-Layl 92:15-16] and His saying: 'Indeed, disgrace today and evil are upon the disbelievers' [an-Nahl 16:27] and so forth. The answer: that these are set aside in their literal sense, due to the definitive texts that the perpetrator of a major sin is not a disbeliever, and the consensus established on that – as previously mentioned – and the Khawarij have deviated from what consensus has been established upon, so they are not to be taken into consideration.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the Khawarij's position that major sinners become disbelievers. The author first states the Sunni practice regarding a fasiq (sinner) who dies without repentance: they do not kill him (i.e., they do not execute him as an apostate), they do not apply the rulings of apostates to him, and they bury him in Muslim cemeteries. Then an objection is implied: does not the Quran call such people 'fasiq' (defiantly disobedient), which some take as disbelief? The author answers that the term 'fasiq' in the relevant verse actually refers to the disbeliever, since disbelief is a form of extreme fisq. The hadiths that seem to condemn sinners harshly are meant as intimidation and deterrence, not as literal takfir. The author supports this by citing the famous hadith that the Prophet told Abu Dharr that even if a person commits adultery and theft, he is still a believer 'despite the nose of Abu Dharr' – an idiomatic expression meaning 'in spite of his disapproval'. Then the author explicitly presents the Khawarij's proofs: they cite verses that equate not judging by Allah's law with disbelief, that link disbelief with fisq, and the hadith that abandoning prayer is disbelief; they also argue that punishment is only for disbelievers. The author responds that all these texts are set aside from their apparent meanings because there are definitive texts and consensus that a major sinner is not a disbeliever. The Khawarij, by contradicting consensus, are themselves deviant and their opinion is disregarded. The passage thus upholds the Sunni doctrine that faith remains with sin, and that repentance can be accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the Khawarij were merely strict but within orthodoxy; they actually held that major sins expel one from Islam entirely.
Correction: The Sunni position, based on definitive texts and consensus, holds that major sins do not expel one from faith; the sinner remains a believer.

Checkpoint

A) He says such verses abrogated.
B) He says the verse refers to the disbeliever, not the Muslim sinner.
C) He says the verse applies only to rulers, not common people.
D) He says the verse means the person is a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij are refuted by showing that their proof texts are not to be taken literally, and the consensus of the Ummah upholds that a major sinner remains a believer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever looked down on a fellow Muslim who committed a major sin, thinking they are 'less of a believer'? How does the Prophet's statement to Abu Dharr—'Even if he commits adultery and steals, he is a believer'—challenge that attitude? Will you ask Allah to forgive them and treat them as a brother/sister starting today?
?
When you read verses like 'Whoever does not judge by what Allah revealed are disbelievers', are you tempted to apply them to others you disagree with? How can you guard your heart from the Khawarij's mistake of taking texts out of context and using them to condemn people? What one habit will you adopt to ensure you interpret the Quran holistically?
?
The Khawarij were known for their extreme piety and devotion, yet they fell into a dangerous error. Is there any area where your zeal for religion leads you to be harsh or judgmental? Confess that to Allah right now and commit to learning from the Sunnah of mercy before making any accusation of kufr.

Clarifying 'After the Prophet' and the Exception of Jesus
Clarifies the meaning of 'after the Prophet' and excludes Jesus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phrase 'after the Prophet' is interpreted in this context and why Jesus must be excluded.

Core Idea
The phrase 'after the Prophet' requires careful qualification to avoid contradiction with Jesus.

The author begins by clarifying whom he means by 'the best of mankind after our Prophet'. He notes that it is better to say 'after the prophets' because no prophet comes after Muhammad. However, he must specify temporal posteriority and exclude Jesus, who will return at the end of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening lines
Read from 'So the best of mankind after our Prophet' up to 'it would be contradicted by Jesus' to grasp the initial qualification.
Notice the phrase 'temporal posteriority'.
2
Consider the hypotheticals
Examine the four possible interpretations of 'every human after our Prophet' and see how each fails.
The author uses logical refutation.
3
Understand the exception of Jesus
Identify why Jesus is excluded despite being a human after the Prophet's time.
The footnote explains his future return.
4
Reflect on the footnote
Read the footnote about Jesus appearing again as a major sign of the Hour.
This clarifies the temporal exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأفضل البشر بعد نبينا، والأحسن أن يقال: بعد الأنبياء. لكنه أراد البعدية الزمانية، وليس بعد نبينا نبي. ومع ذلك لا بد من تخصيص عيسى عليه السلام، إذ لو أريد كل بشر يوجد بعد نبينا انتقض بعيسى عليه السلام، ولو أريد كل بشر يولد بعده، لم يفد التفضيل على الصحابة. ولو أريد كل بشر هو موجود على وجه الأرض لم يفد التفضيل على التابعين ومن بعدهم، ولو أريد كل بشر يوجد على وجه الأرض في الجملة، انتقض بعيسى عليه السلام. أبو بكر الصديق الذي صدق النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في النبوة من غير تلعثم وتردد وفي المعراج بلا تردد. ثم عمر الفاروق الذي فرق بين الحق والباطل في القضايا والخصومات. ثم عثمان ذو النورين لأن النبي عليه السلام زوجه رقية، ولما ماتت رقية زوجه أم كلثوم، ولما ماتت قال: لو كان عندي ثالثة لزوجتكها. ثم علي المرتضى من عباد الله وخالص أصحاب رسول الله. على هذا وجدنا السلف. والظاهر أنه لو لم يكن لهم دليل على ذلك، لما حكموا بذلك. وأما نحن فقد وجدنا دلائل الجانبين متعارضة. ولم نجد هذه المسألة مما يتعلق بها شيء من الأعمال، أو يكون التوقف فيه مخلا بشيء من الواجبات فيها. وكان السلف متوافقين في تفضيل عثمان على علي رضي الله عنه. (1) إذ أنه يظهر مرة أخرى على الأرض وهو من العلامات الكبرى للساعة - وانظر كتابي ( المسيح الدجال وعلامات الساعة الصغرى والكبرى ).
So the best of mankind after our Prophet, and it is better to say: after the prophets. But he intended temporal posteriority, and there is no prophet after our Prophet. Nevertheless, it is necessary to exclude Jesus, peace be upon him; because if every human who exists after our Prophet were intended, it would be contradicted by Jesus, and if every human born after him were intended, it would not benefit the preference over the Companions. And if every human present on the face of the earth were intended, it would not benefit the preference over the Successors and those after them; and if every human who exists on the face of the earth in general were intended, it would be contradicted by Jesus. Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, who believed the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, in prophethood without hesitation or delay, and in the Night Journey without hesitation. Then Umar al-Faruq, who distinguished between truth and falsehood in cases and disputes. Then Uthman Dhu al-Nurayn, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, married him to Ruqayya, and when Ruqayya died, married him to Umm Kulthum, and when she died he said: 'If I had a third, I would have married her to you.' Then Ali al-Murtada, from the servants of God and the sincere companions of the Messenger of God. Upon this we found the Salaf. And it is apparent that if they had no evidence for that, they would not have judged accordingly. But we have found the evidences of the two sides conflicting. And we have not found this matter to involve anything of actions, or that refraining regarding it would be detrimental to any of the obligations in them. And the Salaf were agreed upon preferring Uthman over Ali, may God be pleased with them. (1) For he will appear again on earth and it is one of the major signs of the Hour - and see my book (The Antichrist and the Minor and Major Signs of the Hour).
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage addresses the question of who is the best of mankind after the Prophet. The author first clarifies that 'after' means temporal posteriority, but since no prophet comes after Muhammad, it applies to all humans. However, Jesus is excepted because he will return. Then various interpretations of 'all humans after' are examined and deemed problematic. The author then lists the four caliphs in order: Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali, providing brief justifications for each. He states that this was the way of the Salaf (early generations). However, he adds that the evidence on this ranking is conflicting and the matter has no practical implication for obligations. He notes that the Salaf were agreed on preferring Uthman over Ali. The footnote (which may be an editorial addition) clarifies the exception of Jesus by mentioning his future return as a sign of the Hour.

Key Terms
أفضل البشر
البعدية الزمانية
عيسى عليه السلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'after the Prophet' includes all humans including Jesus.
Correction: The author specifies temporal posteriority and excludes Jesus because he will return.

Checkpoint
Why must Jesus (peace be upon him) be excluded when discussing 'the best of mankind after our Prophet'?
A) He is better than the Prophet
B) He is not human
C) He will return at the end of time
D) He was not a believer

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'after the Prophet' must be understood as temporal posteriority with the exception of Jesus, who will return at the end of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever casually used the phrase 'after the Prophet' without realizing the theological precision it requires? How will you be more careful with your words about faith from now on?
?
The author goes through a detailed logical process to avoid a contradiction. Are you willing to invest that kind of mental effort to defend the truth, or do you settle for simplified statements that might be inaccurate?
?
Jesus is uniquely placed in our theology—beloved yet exceptional. Does your love for him align with the precise boundaries set by revelation, or do you let sentiment blur the lines? What will you do to correct that?

The Ranking of the Four Caliphs and the Salaf's Consensus
Ranks the four caliphs and states the Salaf's consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the traditional Sunni ranking of the four caliphs and the basis for it.

Core Idea
The Salaf agreed on the ranking: Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali.

After clarifying the scope, the author lists the four caliphs in order of merit, providing brief justifications for each. He then notes that the Salaf (early generations) were agreed upon this ranking, even though the evidences are conflicting and the matter has no practical bearing on obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the listing of the four caliphs
Read the passage from 'Abu Bakr al-Siddiq' to 'upon this we found the Salaf' to see the order and justifications.
Notice the titles: al-Siddiq, al-Faruq, Dhu al-Nurayn, al-Murtada.
2
Understand each justification
Identify the specific reason given for each caliph's rank.
Abu Bakr: belief without hesitation; Umar: discernment; Uthman: marriage to two daughters; Ali: closeness.
3
Note the Salaf's consensus
Read the author's statement that the Salaf were agreed on this ranking.
The author also notes conflicting evidence.
4
Reflect on practical implications
Consider the author's remark that this matter has no bearing on actions or obligations.
This shows the issue is not essential.

?

The Exception of Jesus in 'No Prophet After Me'
Sanusi explains the necessary exception of Jesus in the finality of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the phrase 'no prophet after our prophet' must be interpreted as temporal posteriority with the exception of Jesus, and how different interpretations are ruled out.

Core Idea
The 'no prophet after me' statement specifies temporal order, not absolute cessation.

This lesson tackles a subtle point in understanding the finality of prophethood. The author explains that the phrase 'no prophet after our prophet' refers to temporal posteriority, but must be qualified to avoid contradiction with Jesus's return. He then shows why alternative interpretations fail.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on Jesus exception
Focus on the author's logical reasoning: 'ولو أريد...انتقض'. Notice how he systematically eliminates possible interpretations.
2
Understand the core issue
The phrase 'no prophet after me' could imply no prophet in any sense. But Jesus will return, so an exception is needed. The author limits it to temporal posteriority.
3
Analyze the rejected interpretations
Consider each 'if meant' scenario and why it fails. This clarifies the precise meaning intended by the Salaf.
These distinctions safeguard the correct belief while accommodating Jesus's return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنه أراد البعدية الزمانية، وليس بعد نبينا نبي. ومع ذلك لا بد من تخصيص عيسى عليه السلام، إذ لو أريد كل بشر يوجد بعد نبينا انتقض بعيسى عليه السلام ولو أريد كل بشر يولد بعده، لم يفد التفضيل على الصحابة. ولو أريد كل بشر هو موجود على وجه الأرض لم يفد التفضيل على التابعين ومن بعدهم، ولو أريد كل بشر يوجد على وجه الأرض في الجملة، انتقض بعيسى عليه السلام.
أبو بكر الصديق الذي صدق النبي الله في النبوة من غير تلعثم وتردد وفي المعراج بلا تردد. ثم عمر الفاروق الذي فرق بين الحق والباطل في القضايا والخصومات. ثم عثمان ذو النورين لأن النبي عليه السلام زوجه رقية، ولما ماتت رقية زوجه أم كلثوم، ولما ماتت قال: «لو كان عندي ثالثة لزوجتكها». ثم علي المرتضى من عباد الله وخالص أصحاب رسول الله. وعلى هذا وجدنا السلف.
والظاهر أنه لو لم يكن لهم دليل على ذلك، لما حكموا بذلك. وأما نحن فقد وجدنا دلائل الجانبين متعارضة. ولم نجد هذه المسألة مما يتعلق بها شيء من الأعمال، أو يكون التوقف فيه مخلا بشيء من الواجبات فيها.
وكان السلف متوافقين في تفضيل عثمان على علي - رضى الله عنهما - حيث جعلوا من علامات السنة والجماعة تفضيل الشيخين ومحبة الختنين.
والإنصاف: أنه إن أريد بالأفضلية كثرة الثواب، فللتوقف جهة. وإن أريد كثرة ما يعده ذوو العقول من الفضائل، فلا.
But he intended temporal posteriority, and there is no prophet after our prophet. Nevertheless, it is necessary to specify Jesus, peace be upon him, because if one meant every human that exists after our prophet, it would be invalidated by Jesus, peace be upon him; and if one meant every human born after him, it would not benefit the preference over the Companions. And if one meant every human who exists on the face of the earth, it would not benefit the preference over the Successors and those after them; and if one meant every human who exists on the face of the earth in general, it would be invalidated by Jesus, peace be upon him. Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, who believed the Prophet of Allah in prophethood without hesitation or wavering, and in the Ascension without hesitation. Then Umar al-Faruq, who distinguished between truth and falsehood in cases and disputes. Then Uthman Dhu al-Nurayn, because the Prophet, peace be upon him, married him to Ruqayya, and when Ruqayya died, he married him to Umm Kulthum, and when she died, he said: 'If I had a third, I would marry her to you.' Then Ali al-Murtada, from the servants of Allah and the purest of the Companions of the Messenger of Allah. And upon this we found the Salaf. And it is apparent that if they had no evidence for that, they would not have ruled by it. But as for us, we have found the evidences of the two sides contradictory. And we have not found this matter to be related to any deeds, or that hesitation in it would be detrimental to any of the obligatory duties therein. And the Salaf were in agreement in preferring Uthman over Ali - may Allah be pleased with them both - making the preference of the two shaykhs and love of the two sons-in-law among the signs of the Sunna and the Community. And the fair assessment: if by superiority is meant abundance of reward, then there is room for hesitation. And if by it is meant abundance of what people of intellect count as virtues, then not.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage begins by addressing an issue regarding the phrase 'there is no prophet after our prophet.' The author explains that the intended meaning is temporal posteriority, but Jesus (peace be upon him) is an exception because he will return. He then considers different interpretations of 'every human' to show that the intended meaning must be specific in order to avoid contradiction and to support the preference of the Companions. Next, he describes the four caliphs with their distinctive titles: Abu Bakr al-Siddiq who believed in the Prophet without hesitation, Umar al-Faruq who distinguished truth from falsehood, Uthman Dhu al-Nurayn who married two of the Prophet's daughters, and Ali al-Murtada, the purest of the Companions. He states that this is the way of the Salaf. Then he notes that the evidences regarding their relative merits are contradictory, but this issue is not a matter of practical obligation. He then reports that the Salaf agreed on preferring Uthman over Ali, and made loving the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) and the two sons-in-law (Uthman and Ali) a sign of Ahl al-Sunna. Finally, he gives a fair assessment: if by 'superiority' is meant greater reward, then there is room for hesitation; but if it means recognized virtues, then there is no hesitation—meaning the Salaf's view is clear.

Key Terms
بعدية زمانية
استثناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say it is necessary to specify Jesus (peace be upon him) when interpreting 'no prophet after our prophet'?
A) Because Jesus was a prophet before Muhammad.
B) Because Jesus will return after Muhammad, so he must be excepted.
C) Because Jesus is not considered a prophet in Islam.
D) Because the phrase refers only to prophets from the lineage of Israel.

Key Takeaway
The finality of prophethood is temporal: no new prophet will come after Muhammad, but Jesus, who was a prophet before, will return.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever encountered a statement from the Prophet ﷺ that seemed absolute, but you later discovered it had a specific exception like this one? How did that affect your trust in Islamic scholarship?
?
This lesson shows that even a clear phrase like 'no prophet after me' requires careful interpretation to harmonize with other truths. When you come across a Quranic verse or hadith that seems to contradict another, do you rush to reject one, or do you pause and seek out how scholars reconciled them? What will you do differently starting today?
?
If you truly believe that no new prophet will come after Muhammad, do your actions reflect that—or do you sometimes give more weight to modern 'spiritual leaders' who claim divine inspiration? Examine your heart: is there anyone you follow whose authority rivals or surpasses the Prophet's in your daily life?

Virtues of the Four Rightly Guided Caliphs
Sanusi enumerates the four caliphs with their distinct virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the distinctive titles and virtues of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali as described by the author, and understand that this portrayal reflects the view of the Salaf.

Core Idea
The four caliphs are distinguished by unique virtues that became their hallmarks.

In this lesson, the author presents a concise description of the four caliphs, each with a defining trait: Abu Bakr's immediate and unwavering faith, Umar's ability to distinguish truth from falsehood, Uthman's honor of marrying two of the Prophet's daughters, and Ali's special closeness to the Prophet. This summary reflects the consensus of the early Muslim community (Salaf).

Guided Path
1
Read the description of each caliph
Pay attention to the titles: al-Siddiq, al-Faruq, Dhu al-Nurayn, al-Murtada. Note the specific virtue mentioned for each.
These titles are widely recognized in Sunni tradition.
2
Understand the phrase 'upon this we found the Salaf'
This indicates that the earlier generations agreed on the virtues mentioned and their order of merit.
3
Reflect on the example of Uthman's marriages
The Prophet married Uthman to two of his daughters, which is a unique honor. The phrase 'if I had a third' emphasizes the Prophet's affection.

Then Ali al-Murtada, from the servants of Allah and the purest of the Companions of the Messenger of Allah. And upon this we found the Salaf. And it is apparent that if they had no evidence for that, they would not have ruled by it. But as for us, we have found the evidences of the two sides contradictory. And we have not found this matter to be related to any deeds, or that hesitation in it would be detrimental to any of the obligatory duties therein. And the Salaf were in agreement in preferring Uthman over Ali - may Allah be pleased with them both - making the preference of the two shaykhs and love of the two sons-in-law among the signs of the Sunna and the Community. And the fair assessment: if by superiority is meant abundance of reward, then there is room for hesitation. And if by it is meant abundance of what people of intellect count as virtues, then not.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage begins by addressing an issue regarding the phrase 'there is no prophet after our prophet.' The author explains that the intended meaning is temporal posteriority, but Jesus (peace be upon him) is an exception because he will return. He then considers different interpretations of 'every human' to show that the intended meaning must be specific in order to avoid contradiction and to support the preference of the Companions. Next, he describes the four caliphs with their distinctive titles: Abu Bakr al-Siddiq who believed in the Prophet without hesitation, Umar al-Faruq who distinguished truth from falsehood, Uthman Dhu al-Nurayn who married two of the Prophet's daughters, and Ali al-Murtada, the purest of the Companions. He states that this is the way of the Salaf. Then he notes that the evidences regarding their relative merits are contradictory, but this issue is not a matter of practical obligation. He then reports that the Salaf agreed on preferring Uthman over Ali, and made loving the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) and the two sons-in-law (Uthman and Ali) a sign of Ahl al-Sunna. Finally, he gives a fair assessment: if by 'superiority' is meant greater reward, then there is room for hesitation; but if it means recognized virtues, then there is no hesitation—meaning the Salaf's view is clear.

Key Terms
الصديق
الفاروق
ذو النورين
المرتضى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What virtue is specifically associated with Uthman (Dhu al-Nurayn) in this passage?
A) He was the first to accept Islam.
B) He married two of the Prophet's daughters.
C) He compiled the Quran.
D) He was known for his justice.

Key Takeaway
Each of the four caliphs is distinguished by a unique virtue that became his title, and this portrayal represents the established view of the early Muslim community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abu Bakr believed the Prophet without any hesitation, even in the matter of the Ascension. When you hear something from the Quran or Sunnah that challenges your current understanding or lifestyle, do you embrace it immediately or do you find yourself wavering? What one situation today can you practice that level of immediate acceptance?
?
Umar was called 'al-Faruq' because he distinguished truth from falsehood in disputes. Think of a moral or religious dilemma you are currently facing. Are you actively seeking to distinguish the truth, or are you avoiding the clarity it demands? What step can you take today to be a 'faruq' in your own life?
?
The Prophet told Uthman, 'If I had a third daughter, I would marry her to you,' showing immense honor. Do you truly honor the people in your life who have been good to you, or do you take their goodness for granted? Write down one concrete way you will honor someone who has blessed you this week.

Salaf's Agreement and the Meaning of Superiority
Sanusi explains the Salaf's view on preferring Uthman over Ali and clarifies the meaning of superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Salaf's agreement on preferring Uthman over Ali, the author's fair assessment on the meaning of superiority, and why hesitation is allowed if superiority means reward.

Core Idea
Superiority can mean reward or virtues; the Salaf's preference for Uthman concerns virtues, not necessarily reward.

The author now addresses the contentious issue of ranking the caliphs. He notes that the Salaf (early scholars) were unanimous in preferring Uthman over Ali, and made this a sign of Ahl al-Sunna. However, he also offers a nuanced clarification: if 'superiority' means greater reward, there is room for hesitation; if it means recognized virtues, then the Salaf's view stands firm.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Salaf's agreement and the fair assessment
Notice the shift from reporting the Salaf's view to the author's own analysis. The key sentence is the final one on 'al-insaf'.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'superiority'
The author distinguishes between superiority in reward (thawab) and superiority in worldly recognized virtues. This distinction resolves the apparent tension.
This is a subtle but important point for avoiding extremism.
3
Appreciate the practical implication
The author says this issue does not affect any obligatory actions, so it is not necessary to have a definitive stance.
This encourages unity and tolerance in matters of ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنه أراد البعدية الزمانية، وليس بعد نبينا نبي. ومع ذلك لا بد من تخصيص عيسى عليه السلام، إذ لو أريد كل بشر يوجد بعد نبينا انتقض بعيسى عليه السلام ولو أريد كل بشر يولد بعده، لم يفد التفضيل على الصحابة. ولو أريد كل بشر هو موجود على وجه الأرض لم يفد التفضيل على التابعين ومن بعدهم، ولو أريد كل بشر يوجد على وجه الأرض في الجملة، انتقض بعيسى عليه السلام.
أبو بكر الصديق الذي صدق النبي الله في النبوة من غير تلعثم وتردد وفي المعراج بلا تردد. ثم عمر الفاروق الذي فرق بين الحق والباطل في القضايا والخصومات. ثم عثمان ذو النورين لأن النبي عليه السلام زوجه رقية، ولما ماتت رقية زوجه أم كلثوم، ولما ماتت قال: «لو كان عندي ثالثة لزوجتكها». ثم علي المرتضى من عباد الله وخالص أصحاب رسول الله. وعلى هذا وجدنا السلف.

?

?

Proving the Caliphate Order: Consensus and Historical Evidence
Definition and historical proof for caliphate order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sunni scholars establish the caliphate order of the four caliphs through the consensus of the Companions and historical evidence from the Saqifa event and subsequent pledges.

Core Idea
The caliphate order is proven by the unanimous consensus of the Companions, not by an explicit text.

After establishing the core beliefs about the Companions, the author now addresses the historical succession of the caliphate. Using the well-known events at the Saqifa of Banu Sa'ida and the subsequent pledge of allegiance from Ali, he provides a rational and historical proof for the order of the first four caliphs. This passage also refutes the Shi'a claim of an explicit textual designation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of caliphate
Start by reading the opening definition: caliphate is deputyship from the Messenger in establishing the religion, making it obligatory for all nations to follow.
This sets the theological weight of the office.
2
Read the Saqifa account
Read the description of the Companions gathering after the Prophet's death, their consultation and contention, and their eventual consensus on Abu Bakr.
Notice the process of consultation and agreement.
3
Examine Ali's pledge
The author mentions that Ali pledged allegiance publicly after hesitation. Ask: What does Ali's acceptance imply about the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate?
Ali's action refutes the claim of a secret text.
4
Reflect on the argument against false consensus
The author argues that if the caliphate were not rightful, the Companions would not have agreed and Ali would have contested it. Consider why this is a strong argument.
It appeals to the integrity of the Companions.
5
Consider the appointment of Umar
Read how Abu Bakr, on his deathbed, dictated a document appointing Umar, which was accepted by the people. This shows the continuity of the caliphate through consultation and appointment.
This establishes the precedent for selection by the previous caliph.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخلافتهم أى نيابتهم عن الرسول في إقامة الدين بحيث يجب على كافة الأمم الاتباع. على هذا الترتيب أيضا، يعنى أن الخلافة بعد رسول الله لأبي بكر ثم لعمر ثم لعثمان ثم لعلى - رضى الله عنهم -. وذلك لأن الصحابة قد اجتمعوا يوم توفى رسول الله في سقيفة بني ساعدة واستقر رأيهم بعد المشاورة والمنازعة على خلافة أبي بكر - رضى الله عنه - وأجمعوا على ذلك. وبايعه على - رضى الله عنه على رؤوس الأشهاد بعد توقف كان منه. ولو لم تكن الخلافة حقا له لما اتفق عليه الصحابة، ولنازعه على رضي الله عنه، كما نازع معاوية ولاحتج عليه لو كان في حقه نص كما زعمت الشيعة وكيف يتصور في حق أصحاب رسول الله الاتفاق على الباطل، وترك العمل بالنص الوارد؟ ثم إن أبا بكر رضى الله عنه لما أيس من حياته، دعا عثمان رضى الله عنه وأملى عليه كتاب عهده لعمر رضى الله عنه، فلما كتب عثمان، ختم الصحيفة وأخرجها إلى الناس وأمرهم أن يبايعوا لمن في
And their caliphate, meaning their deputyship from the Messenger in establishing the religion such that it is obligatory upon all nations to follow. In this order as well, meaning that the caliphate after the Messenger of Allah is for Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali - may Allah be pleased with them. That is because the Companions gathered on the day the Messenger of Allah died in the portico of Banu Sa'ida, and their opinion settled after consultation and contention on the caliphate of Abu Bakr - may Allah be pleased with him - and they unanimously agreed upon that. And Ali - may Allah be pleased with him - gave him the pledge of allegiance publicly after a hesitation that came from him. And if the caliphate were not rightful for him, the Companions would not have agreed upon it, and Ali - may Allah be pleased with him - would have disputed it, just as Mu'awiya disputed, and he would have argued against him if there had been a text regarding his right as the Shi'a claim. And how can it be imagined of the Companions of the Messenger of Allah to agree upon falsehood and abandon acting upon a transmitted text? Then, when Abu Bakr - may Allah be pleased with him - despaired of his life, he called Uthman - may Allah be pleased with him - and dictated to him a document of his covenant for Umar - may Allah be pleased with him. So when Uthman wrote it, he sealed the document, brought it out to the people, and ordered them to pledge allegiance to the one in
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the order of the caliphate. The author begins by defining the caliphate as deputyship from the Messenger in establishing the religion, incumbent on all nations. He then asserts the well-known order: Abu Bakr, then Umar, then Uthman, then Ali. He provides evidence from historical events. First, the Companions gathered at the Saqifa of Banu Sa'ida after the Prophet's death, and after discussion and initial contention, they agreed on Abu Bakr's caliphate. Second, Ali himself pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr publicly after a brief hesitation, which shows that Ali accepted the consensus. The author argues that if the caliphate were not rightfully Abu Bakr's, the Companions would not have agreed upon it, and Ali would have contested it just as Mu'awiya later contested, and if there had been a clear text (nass) designating Ali as the immediate successor, Ali would have used it against Abu Bakr. The author considers it impossible that the Companions could unanimously agree on falsehood and ignore a transmitted text. Finally, he mentions that Abu Bakr, when near death, appointed Umar through a written document dictated to Uthman, thereby establishing the principle of selection by the previous caliph. This supports the legitimacy of the caliphate order.

Key Terms
خلافة
نيابة
إجماع
نص
سقيفة بني ساعدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The caliphate order is based on an explicit text from the Prophet designating Ali as immediate successor.
Correction: The Sunni position holds that the order was established by the consensus of the Companions at Saqifa and subsequent historical events, not by a single textual designation. Ali's public pledge to Abu Bakr disproves the claim of a hidden text.

Checkpoint
What main evidence does the author provide for the caliphate order of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali?
A) An explicit text from the Quran.
B) The consensus of the Companions at Saqifa and Ali's pledge.
C) A hadith from the Prophet.
D) The agreement of the Umayyad dynasty.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position on the caliphate order is grounded in the historical consensus of the Companions, not in a single textual designation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The source argues that the Companions cannot have agreed on falsehood. Do you truly trust their collective judgment more than a single text that might support your preferred candidate? Are you willing to submit your own opinion to the historical consensus of the Companions, even if it challenges your internal biases?
?
If you were in Ali's position, having hesitated but then pledged allegiance to Abu Bakr, what would have motivated you to put aside any personal claim for the unity of the community? Can you point to a time when you had to submit to a decision you initially disagreed with for the greater good of the group?
?
The passage forces you to confront the possibility that your belief about Ali's right might be based on a claim that even he himself did not act upon. How does that challenge your assumptions about early Islamic history? Will you seek to realign your convictions with the evidence presented here, or will you cling to inherited narratives despite the historical argument?

The Signs of Ahl al-Sunnah and the Meaning of Preference
Definition of the Sunni sign and analysis of 'preference'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the author means by 'preference' of the caliphs and why he distinguishes between two possible interpretations of the term.

Core Idea
Preferring the caliphs in virtue is a sign of Sunni orthodoxy.

The author begins by listing one of the signs of Ahl al-Sunnah: preferring Abu Bakr and Umar and loving Uthman and Ali. He then clarifies what 'preference' means, noting that if it refers to greater reward, there can be hesitation, but if it refers to virtues, then there is no doubt. This sets the stage for defending the Sunni order of the caliphs.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'عنهما - حيث جعلوا...' and note the claim that preferring the two shaykhs and loving the two sons-in-law is a sign of Ahl al-Sunnah.
This is a positive statement of Sunni identity.
2
Understand the distinction in 'preference'
The author discusses two interpretations of 'preference': one about reward, another about virtues. Note the hesitation in the first case and the certainty in the second.
The term 'أفضلية' is key.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the author makes this distinction. It allows a nuanced position: acknowledging the virtues of all caliphs while avoiding absolutism about reward.
This paves the way for the historical arguments that follow.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

From them - where they made among the signs of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah the preference of the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) and love of the two sons-in-law (Uthman and Ali). He chose Uthman and pledged allegiance to him in the presence of the Companions, so they pledged allegiance and submitted to his orders and prohibitions, and prayed with him the Friday and Eid prayers, so it was a consensus. Then he was martyred and left the matter neglected. So the senior Emigrants and Helpers gathered around Ali - may God be pleased with him - and sought from him acceptance of the caliphate, and they pledged allegiance to him, because he was the most virtuous of the people of his time and the most entitled to the caliphate. And what occurred of disagreements and battles was not due to dispute over his caliphate, but due to error in ijtihad. And what occurred of disagreement between the Shia and Ahl al-Sunnah in this matter, and the claim of each of the two groups of a text concerning imamate, and the presentation of questions and answers from both sides, is mentioned in the lengthy works.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Sunni order of the caliphs. The author begins by noting that among the signs of Ahl al-Sunnah is preferring the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar) and loving the two sons-in-law (Uthman and Ali). He then discusses the meaning of preference: if by preference is meant more reward, there is hesitation; if it means more virtues, then no. Next, he explains the caliphate in order: Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali. He gives historical proofs: the Companions gathered after the Prophet's death and agreed on Abu Bakr, and Ali himself pledged allegiance after a delay. If there had been a text for Ali, the Companions would not have agreed on someone else, and Ali would have protested, but he did not. Then depicts the succession of Umar by Abu Bakr's appointment, Uthman by shura, and Ali by consensus after Uthman's death. The author argues that conflicts during Ali's caliphate were due to ijtihad errors, not disputes over his legitimacy. Finally, he notes that the detailed arguments between Shia and Sunni are in lengthy works.

Key Terms
الشيخين
الختنين
أفضلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Sunni doctrine requires absolute certainty that Abu Bakr and Umar have more reward than Ali in the afterlife.
Correction: The author clarifies that if 'preference' is about reward, there is room for hesitation; the certainty is about their virtues as commonly recognized.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's position on the preference of Abu Bakr and Umar over Ali in terms of reward?
A) It is certain.
B) There is hesitation.
C) It is denied.
D) It is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The author distinguishes between preference in virtue, which is affirmed, and preference in reward, which is left to Allah's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate people—whether scholars, leaders, or friends—do you base your judgment on their observable virtues or on what you assume about their standing with Allah? How does the author's hesitation about divine reward challenge your own tendency to rank people in your heart?
?
The author says that preference in virtue is something that 'reasonable people' can agree upon. Are you willing to apply this same standard to yourself: Do you seek to develop virtues that are recognized by sound reason, or do you rely on claims of spiritual status? Where do you most need to cultivate the virtues the author speaks of?
?
If you were asked to defend the Sunni position on the superiority of the first two caliphs, would you confidently affirm their precedence without hesitation? Or would you pause, like the author, recognizing that ultimate reward is known only to Allah? In your heart, do you accept this nuance, or do you feel the need to assert absolute certainty?
