Conditions for the Imam
Enumeration of imam conditions with justifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the necessary qualifications for the imam in Sunni political theology and the reasoning behind each condition.

Core Idea
The imam must possess complete authority and capability to lead the community.

This passage from the Sharh al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyyah enumerates the essential qualifications for the imam. These conditions are grounded in both scriptural principle and practical necessity. Let's examine each requirement and its justification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation to grasp the author's enumeration and justifications.
Notice the compact style: each condition is given with a brief rationale.
2
Identify the seven conditions
List the specific conditions mentioned: Muslim, free, male, rational, adult, sā’is (polity-conducting), qādir (capable).
The first five are unpacked from 'absolute guardianship'; the last two are added explicitly.
3
Understand the justifications
For each condition, note the reasoning provided: e.g., Muslim because disbelievers cannot have authority over believers; slave is occupied and despised; women deficient in intellect and religion; minors/insane incapable; sā’is requires sound opinion and military power; qādir requires knowledge, justice, sufficiency, bravery.
Some justifications are scriptural, others rational.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why these conditions are necessary: they ensure the imam can execute rulings, protect borders, and ensure justice. Neglecting them undermines the very purpose of the imamate.
This connects the conditions to the goal of the imam's office.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشترط أن يكون من أهل الولاية المطلقة الكاملة) أى مسلما حرا ذكرا عاقلا بالغا ، إذ ما جعل الله للكافرين على المؤمنين سبيلا والعبد مشغول بخدمة المولى مستحقر في أعين الناس. والنساء ناقصات عقل ودين. والصبي والمجنون قاصران عن تدبير الأمور والتصرف في مصالح الجمهور. (سائسا) أى مالكا للتصرف فى أمور المسلمين بقوة رأيه ورويته ومعونة بأسه وشوكته. (قادرا ) بعلمه وعدله وكفايته وشجاعته على تنفيذ الأحكام، وحفظ حدود دار الإسلام، وإنصاف المظلوم من الظالم إذ الإخلال بهذه الأمور مخل بالغرض من نصب الإمام .
And it is required that he be of complete and absolute guardianship (wilāya), i.e., Muslim, free, male, rational, adult, because Allah has not made for the disbelievers any way over the believers, and the slave is occupied with serving his master and despised in the eyes of people. And women are deficient in intellect and religion. And the boy and the insane are incapable of managing affairs and disposing of the interests of the public. (Polity-conducting) i.e., possessing authority to manage the affairs of the Muslims by the strength of his opinion and his deliberation and the help of his might and power. (Capable) i.e., by his knowledge, justice, sufficiency, and bravery, to execute the rulings, preserve the borders of the House of Islam, and give justice to the oppressed from the oppressor, because neglecting these matters is detrimental to the purpose of appointing the imam.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the conditions (shurūṭ) for the imam. First, the imam must be a person of complete and absolute guardianship (wilāya), which is then broken down into specific requirements: Muslim, free, male, rational, adult. Each condition is justified: the Quranic principle that disbelievers cannot have authority over believers; the slave is occupied with service and is despised; women are deficient in intellect and religion; boys and the insane are incapable of public administration. Then two additional conditions follow: he must be a polity-conducting leader (sā’is), meaning he has authority to manage affairs with sound opinion and deliberation backed by military power; and he must be capable (qādir), possessing knowledge, justice, self-sufficiency, and bravery to execute rulings, protect the borders of the Islamic state, and ensure justice. Neglecting these essentials undermines the very purpose of appointing an imam.

Misconception: That being male and free are not necessary conditions for imamate according to some views.
Correction: This passage affirms these as necessary conditions based on the classical Sunni position.

Checkpoint

A) Being male.
B) Being a mujtahid.
C) Being rational.
D) Being capable (qādir).

Key Takeaway
The imam must possess both religious and practical qualifications—being Muslim, free, male, rational, adult, a capable leader, and possessing knowledge, justice, and bravery—to effectively govern and uphold the Sharia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the condition that the imam must be 'قادرا' (capable) with knowledge, justice, and bravery, ask yourself: In your own sphere of influence, are you striving to cultivate these qualities? Or do you excuse your negligence by thinking leadership is only for some? How will you develop at least one of these attributes starting today?
?
The passage says neglecting these conditions undermines the purpose of appointing an imam. Reflect on the leaders you follow—whether in your community or online. Do they meet these criteria? If not, what is your responsibility? Will you continue to blindly follow or seek qualified guidance?
?
The text justifies certain conditions by citing inherent deficiencies (e.g., women being deficient in intellect and religion). This challenges modern assumptions. Can you submit to Allah's wisdom even when it contradicts societal norms? What change in your heart is needed to accept divine decree over personal opinion?

The Imam Need Not Be the Most Virtuous; The Validity of Shura
Argues that the imam need not be the most virtuous, citing Umar's shura.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the imam does not have to be the most virtuous person and how shura (consultation) among a group can be considered a single imamate.

Core Idea
Leadership requires practical wisdom, not just personal virtue.

In this passage, the author addresses a common assumption: that the imam must be the most virtuous person of his time. He argues that practical governance requires different qualities. He supports this with the example of Umar's shura and also answers a potential objection about multiple imams.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the objection and answer at the end.
2
Identify the main argument and example
The author states that the imam need not be the most virtuous. Find the reason given and the example of Umar's shura.
Note how the author distinguishes between virtue and governance capability.
3
Understand the objection and its answer
An objection is raised about the permissibility of shura among six. How does the author resolve the apparent conflict with the rule of one imam?
The key is the distinction between independent imams and a consultative body acting as one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا أن يكون أفضل أهل زمانه لأن المساوى في الفضيلة بل المفضول الأقل علما وعملا، ربما كان أعرف بمصالح الإمامة ومفاسدها، وأقدر على القيام بمواجبها، خصوصا إذا كان نصب المفضول أدفع للشر، وأبعد عن إثارة الفتنة ولهذا جعل عمر - رضى عنه - الإمامة شورى بين ستة مع القطع بأن بعضهم أفضل من بعض . فإن قيل: كيف صح جعل الإمامة شورى بين الستة مع أنه لا يجوز نصب إمامين في زمان واحد . قلنا : غير الجائز هو نصب إمامين مستقلين يجب طاعة كل منهما على الانفراد لما يلزم من ذلك من امتثال أحكام متضادة . وأما في الشورى فالكل بمنزلة إمام واحد
And it is not required that he be the most virtuous of his time, because one equal in virtue—rather the less virtuous who has less knowledge and action—may be more aware of the interests of the imamate and its corruptions, and more capable of fulfilling its duties, especially if appointing the less virtuous is more repelling of evil and further from inciting sedition. For this reason, Umar—may God be pleased with him—made the imamate a consultation (shura) among six, while being certain that some of them were better than others. If it is said: How is it valid to make the imamate a consultation among six, given that it is not permissible to appoint two imams at one time? We say: What is not permissible is appointing two independent imams, each to be obeyed individually, because that would necessitate obeying contradictory rulings. As for consultation (shura), all of them are in the position of a single imam.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage argues that the imam need not be the most virtuous person of his time. The author explains that a person of lesser virtue, or even one inferior in knowledge and action, may be better suited for governance because he might be more knowledgeable about the practical interests of the imamate and more capable of executing its duties. This is especially true if appointing the less virtuous person serves to repel evil and avoid sedition. The author cites the example of Umar (may God be pleased with him) who made the imamate a matter of consultation (shura) among six people, despite the fact that some of them were superior to others. Then the author anticipates an objection: How can consultation among six be valid when it is not permissible to have two imams at the same time? He answers by distinguishing between two independent imams—each to be obeyed individually, which would lead to contradictory commands—and consultation, where all six together are like a single imam. Thus, shura does not violate the rule against multiple imams.

Key Terms

المفضول
الشورى
إمامين
الانفراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imam must be the most virtuous person in every aspect.
Correction: The author argues that practical governance capability and avoidance of sedition can outweigh personal virtue, so the imam may be less virtuous.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the imam need not be the most virtuous?
A) Because virtue is not important for leadership.
B) Because a less virtuous person might be more capable of governance and avoiding sedition.
C) Because Umar appointed the most virtuous six.
D) Because the imam must be sinless.

Key Takeaway
The imam need not be the most virtuous; practical governance and preventing sedition are more important, and shura is valid as it functions as one imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate a leader, do you prioritize their personal virtue or their ability to maintain peace and order? Which has been more important in your own experience, and why?
?
If you were part of a shura council, would you be able to accept that someone less virtuous than you might be more qualified to lead? What would that reveal about your humility?
?
The passage argues that appointing the less virtuous can be better to avoid fitna. Is there a situation in your life where you chose a less 'perfect' option to avoid greater harm? Reflect on that decision and what it taught you.

Conditions for the Imam: Wilaya, Sa'is, and Qadir
Enumerating conditions for the imam: from ahl al-wilaya and additional qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific conditions required for the imam, including the foundational attributes from ahl al-wilaya and the additional qualities of sa'is and qadir.

Core Idea
The imam must meet clear conditions: Muslim, free, male, adult, sane, capable, and just.

This passage from the Sharh al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyyah outlines the essential conditions for the imam. The author begins by stating that the imam must be from 'ahl al-wilaya al-mutlaqa al-kamila'—people of absolute and complete authority. He then explains this as requiring five basic attributes and adds two further qualities that ensure effective leadership. Understanding these conditions is crucial for grasping the Sunni view of legitimate leadership.

Guided Path
1
Understand ahl al-wilaya
Read the opening phrase and its explanation. Notice that the author defines 'absolute authority' through five specific attributes.
These attributes are not arbitrary; each has a rationale.
2
Identify the five basic conditions
List the five conditions: Muslim, free, male, adult, sane. Reflect on why each is necessary according to the author's reasoning.
The reasons are given in the commentary: for non-Muslims, no authority over believers; for slaves, occupied and despised; women deficient in intellect and religion; boys/insane incapable of managing affairs.
3
Recognize the additional conditions
Note the two further qualities: sa'is (manager/director) and qadir (capable). Understand what each entails: sa'is requires strength of opinion and military might; qadir requires knowledge, justice, sufficiency, and courage.
These ensure the imam can execute judgments, protect borders, and deliver justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويشترط أن يكون من أهل الولاية المطلقة الكاملة) أى مسلما حرا ذكرا عاقلا بالغا ، إذ ما جعل الله للكافرين على المؤمنين سبيلا والعبد مشغول بخدمة المولى مستحقر في أعين الناس. والنساء ناقصات عقل ودين. والصبي والمجنون قاصران عن تدبير الأمور والتصرف في مصالح الجمهور. (سائسا) أى مالكا للتصرف فى أمور المسلمين بقوة رأيه ورويته ومعونة بأسه وشوكته. (قادرا ) بعلمه وعدله وكفايته وشجاعته على تنفيذ الأحكام، وحفظ حدود دار الإسلام، وإنصاف المظلوم من الظالم إذ الإخلال بهذه الأمور مخل بالغرض من نصب الإمام .
And it is a condition that he be from the people of absolute and complete authority (al-wilaya al-mutlaqa al-kamila): meaning a Muslim, free, male, adult, sane, because God has not given the disbelievers any way over the believers, and the slave is occupied with serving his master and is contemptible in people's eyes. Women are deficient in intellect and religion. The boy and the insane are incapable of managing affairs and conducting the interests of the populace. (Sa'is): meaning one who possesses discretion in the affairs of Muslims through the strength of his opinion, his deliberation, and the support of his might and power. (Qadir): meaning capable, through his knowledge, justice, sufficiency, and courage, of executing judgments, preserving the borders of the Abode of Islam, and giving justice to the oppressed from the oppressor, because failing in these matters undermines the purpose of appointing the imam.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage enumerates the conditions (shurut) for the imam. The author states that the imam must be from 'ahl al-wilaya al-mutlaqa al-kamila' (people of absolute and complete authority). This is then explained as requiring the imam to be a Muslim, free, male, adult, and sane. The author gives reasons for each: for non-Muslims, because God has not given them authority over believers; for slaves, because they are occupied with serving their master and are looked down upon; for women, because they are deficient in intellect and religion; for boys and the insane, because they are incapable of managing affairs. Then two additional qualities are given: sa'is (ruler or manager), meaning one who has the ability to direct the affairs of Muslims with sound opinion, deliberation, and backed by military force and authority; and qadir (capable), meaning one who possesses knowledge, justice, sufficiency, and courage to execute judgments, protect the borders, and give justice. The author concludes that failing in these matters would undermine the very purpose of appointing the imam.

How will you break free?
?

The Imam Is Not Removed by Sin or Injustice
A ruling that the imam is not removed by sin or injustice, with evidence from the salaf and a comparison of Hanafi and Shafi'i views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on not removing an imam due to sin or injustice, the evidence from the salaf, and the differing views of Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Even sinful rulers are not to be deposed, to avoid sedition.

This lesson addresses a critical practical question: what should Muslims do if their imam commits sins or acts unjustly? The author presents the Sunni consensus that such an imam is not removed from office, citing the practice of the salaf after the Rightly-Guided Caliphs. He also contrasts the views of Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i, highlighting the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully and its English translation. Notice the structure: first the ruling, then the evidence, then the scholarly disagreement.
The passage begins with a clear ruling.
2
Identify the evidence from the salaf
The author says that despite injustice after the Rightly-Guided Caliphs, the early Muslims continued to follow the imams and perform prayers with their permission, and did not rebel. Why was this?
This shows the principle of avoiding sedition.
3
Contrast the two scholars' views
Abu Hanifa holds that a sinful person can still hold authority, while al-Shafi'i says he cannot. Notice the example of the father marrying his daughter. How does this relate to the imam?
The key difference is whether a sinner can be a guardian (wali).
4
Understand the distinction between imam and judge
Even in the Shafi'i school, the imam is not removed by sin because his removal would cause sedition, but a judge can be removed. This shows the practical consideration of maintaining order.
Power (shawka) is a factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينعزل الإمام بالفسق أى بالخروج عن طاعة الله تعالى . (والجور) أى الظلم على عباد الله تعالى، لأنه قد ظهر الفسق وانتشر الجور من الأئمة والأمراء بعد الخلفاء الراشدين والسلف قد كانوا ينقادون لهم، ويقيمون الجمع والأعياد، بإذنهم، ولا يرون الخروج عليهم، ولأن العصمة ليست بشرط للإمامة ابتداء، فببقاء أولى . وعن الشافعي - رحمه الله - إن الإمام ينعزل بالفسق والجور، وكذا كل قاض وأمير وأصل المسألة : أن الفاسق ليس من أهل الولاية عند الشافعي - رحمه الله - لأنه لا ينظر لنفسه، فكيف ينظر لغيره ؟ . وعند أبي حنيفة - رحمه الله - هو من أهل الولاية، حتى يصح للأب الفاسق تزويج ابنته الصغيرة. والمسطور في كتب الشافعية : أن القاضى ينعزل بالفسق، بخلاف الإمام . والفرق : أن فى انعزاله ووجوب نصب غيره، إثارة الفتنة لما له من الشوكة ، بخلاف القاضي .
And the imam is not removed from office by fisq (sin, i.e., departing from obedience to God Almighty) nor by jaur (injustice, i.e., oppression of God's servants), because sin became apparent and injustice spread among imams and governors after the Rightly-Guided Caliphs, yet the predecessors (salaf) used to follow them and lead the Friday prayers and the Eid prayers with their permission, and they did not see fit to rebel against them. Also because infallibility (isma) is not a condition for the imamate at the outset, so its remaining is more appropriate. And it is reported from al-Shafi'i—may God have mercy on him—that the imam is removed by sin and injustice, and likewise every judge (qadi) and governor (amir). The basis of the issue is that the sinful person is not from the people of authority (ahl al-wilaya) according to al-Shafi'i—may God have mercy on him—because he does not look after himself, so how can he look after others? According to Abu Hanifa—may God have mercy on him—he is from the people of authority, so much so that a sinful father's marriage of his minor daughter is valid. And it is recorded in the Shafi'i books that the judge is removed by sin, unlike the imam. The difference is that in his removal and the necessity of appointing another, there is incitement of sedition because of his power, unlike the judge.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage argues that the imam is not removed from office due to fisq (sin) or jaur (injustice). The author provides evidence: after the Rightly-Guided Caliphs, sin and injustice became widespread among imams and governors, yet the early Muslims (salaf) continued to obey them, performed Friday and Eid prayers with their permission, and did not rebel against them. Furthermore, infallibility (isma) is not a condition for the imamate at the time of appointment, so it is more appropriate that it not be a condition for remaining in office. The author then presents the views of two major scholars: al-Shafi'i (may God have mercy on him) held that the imam is removed by sin and injustice, and similarly any judge or governor, because a sinful person is not qualified for authority since he cannot even manage himself. Abu Hanifa (may God have mercy on him) held that a sinful person is still qualified for authority, even giving the example that a sinful father's marriage of his minor daughter is valid. The Shafi'i school's books state that a judge is removed by sin, unlike the imam. The difference is that removing the imam and appointing another would incite sedition due to his power, whereas removing a judge does not cause such sedition.

Key Terms
الفسق
الجور
العصمة
الولاية
الشوكة
الفتنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any sin or injustice automatically disqualifies an imam and requires his removal.
Correction: The Sunni position is that the imam is not removed by sin or injustice, following the precedent of the salaf and to avoid sedition.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why did the salaf not rebel against sinful imams?
A) Because they agreed with their sins.
B) Because they had no power to do so.
C) Because they continued to follow them and perform prayers with their permission, and did not see rebellion as correct.
D) Because the imams were infallible.

Key Takeaway
The imam is not removed due to sin or injustice, as the salaf followed sinful rulers and removing him would cause sedition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time you saw someone in authority (a leader, parent, or teacher) commit a serious wrong. Your first instinct might have been to confront, expose, or remove them. But this lesson teaches a different priority: avoidance of fitna. What would it cost you personally to submit rather than rebel? Are you willing to bear that cost for the sake of community peace? Choose one relationship where you can practice forbearance this week, even if you feel justice demands action.
?
The salaf followed sinful rulers and prayed behind them. How deep is your attachment to your local religious community? If your imam or mosque board committed a major sin, would you still attend the Friday prayer? Where do you draw the line between loyalty to the community and personal moral integrity? Commit to one specific boundary that you will not cross, even in difficult circumstances.
?
The Shafi'i exception shows that the imam's removal causes sedition because of his power. Now, think about your own influence—in your family, workplace, or social circle. When you see injustice, does your ego push you to 'remove' someone from a role? How can you apply the principle of weighing the greater good (avoiding fitna) before acting? What would change in your approach if you truly believed that even a just action could cause more harm than good?

Rulings on a Sinful or Bribed Judge
Three rulings on a corrupt judge from Hanafi sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Hanafi scholars distinguish the validity of a judge's appointment and judgments based on the timing of sinfulness and the presence of bribery.

Core Idea
A judge's authority depends on the integrity of his appointment and freedom from bribery.

This passage from the Sharh al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyyah presents three additional rulings on a corrupt judge. It discusses when a sinful person can validly judge, when a previously upright judge becomes disqualified by sin, and the effect of bribery on the validity of judgments and the judgeship itself. These rulings clarify the importance of integrity in the judiciary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the three distinct opinions presented.
Pay attention to the wording: 'la yajuz', 'yasihhu', 'yun'adhal', etc.
2
Identify the three scenarios
The passage covers: (1) the general principle that a sinful person's judgment is invalid, (2) difference between initial appointment as sinful vs. becoming sinful later, and (3) bribery's effect on specific judgments and the judgeship itself.
Each scenario has a different ruling.
3
Understand the rationale behind scenario 2
Some shaykhs say that if a judge was upright when appointed and later becomes sinful, he is removed because the appointing authority relied on his uprightness. This shows the importance of the condition of the judge at the time of appointment.
4
Learn the consensus on bribery
From Fatawa Qadi Khan, there is agreement that a bribed judge's judgment in that matter is not executed, and if the judgeship itself was obtained by bribery, the person never becomes a valid judge.
This underscores the severity of bribery.
5
Reflect on application
Consider how these rulings would be applied in a contemporary context to ensure justice and integrity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفى رواية النوادر عن العلماء الثلاثة : أنه لا يجوز قضاء الفاسق . وقال بعض المشايخ: إذا قلد الفاسق ابتداء يصح، ولو قلد وهو عدل ينعزل بالفسق، لأن المقلد أعتمد عدالته فلم يرض بقضائه بدونها. وفى فتاوی قاضی خان أجمعوا على أنه إذا ارتشي لا ينفذ قضاؤه فيما ارتشى وأنه إذا أخذ القاضي القضاء بالرشوة لا يصير قاضيا ولو قضى لا ينفذ قضاؤه.
And in the narration of al-Nawadir from the three scholars: that the judgment of a sinful person (fasiq) is not permissible. And some shaykhs said: if a sinful person is appointed initially, it is valid; but if he was appointed while he was upright ('adl) and then becomes sinful, he is removed by sin, because the one who appointed him relied on his uprightness and is not satisfied with his judgment without it. And in the Fatawa of Qadi Khan: they agreed that if he takes a bribe, his judgment in that matter is not executed; and that if the judge takes the judgeship by bribery, he does not become a judge, and even if he passes a judgment, it is not executed.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage provides additional rulings concerning a corrupt judge (qadi). First, it cites a narration from 'al-Nawadir' that the three scholars (presumably Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani) held that the judgment of a sinful person (fasiq) is not permissible (la yajuz). Then it reports the opinion of some shaykhs: if a sinful person is appointed as a judge initially, his appointment is valid (meaning he can judge); but if a person who was upright ('adl) at the time of appointment later becomes sinful, he is removed from office by that sin, because the person who appointed him relied on his uprightness and is not satisfied with his judgment without it. Finally, from the Fatawa of Qadi Khan, there is a consensus (ajma'u) that if a judge takes a bribe (irtasha), his judgment concerning the matter of the bribe is not executed (la yunfadh qada'uhu). Moreover, if a person obtains the judgeship through bribery, he does not actually become a judge, and any judgment he passes is not executed.

Key Terms
الفاسق
العدل
الرشوة
الفقض
التقليد
الانعزال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think a judge's appointment is always valid regardless of how he obtained it or his moral state.
Correction: This passage shows that bribery in obtaining the judgeship invalidates the appointment entirely, and becoming sinful after a valid appointment can lead to automatic removal.

Checkpoint
What happens if a judge takes a bribe according to Fatawa Qadi Khan?
A) His judgment is still valid but he is removed.
B) His judgment in that matter is not executed.
C) He is considered a judge until removed by sultan.
D) His previous judgments are nullified.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a judge's appointment and judgments is conditional on the integrity of the judge at appointment and absence of bribery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a judge is removed by sin because his appointer relied on his uprightness, how does this challenge you to examine your own reliability in positions of trust? Are you currently trusted for a quality you have compromised?
?
The source says a judge who takes a bribe for a case sees that judgment void. Think of a time you accepted a small 'favor' that influenced a decision. How can you restore the integrity of that decision, even if no one noticed?
?
Obtaining judgeship by bribery nullifies the entire judgeship. What 'position' in your life did you gain through less-than-honest means? What would it mean to walk away from that position to honor truth?

The Imam is Not Removed by Sin: The Sunni Position
Author states the position and provides evidence from history and logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that an imam is not removed due to sin or injustice, and the evidence from the practice of the salaf and the reasoning about infallibility.

Core Idea
Sin does not disqualify a ruler; stability and avoiding fitna override.

This lesson presents the foundational Sunni view on the imam's tenure. The author argues that even if a ruler becomes sinful, he is not automatically removed. This position is supported by the precedent of the early Muslim community after the Rightly Guided Caliphs and a logical argument about infallibility not being a condition for imamate at its inception.

Guided Path
1
Read the main statement and definitions
Begin by reading the first sentence where the author states that the imam is not removed by sin or injustice. Note how he defines these terms.
sin = going against Allah, injustice = oppression against people.
2
Examine the historical evidence
Read the part about the appearance of sin after the Rashidun caliphs and how the salaf submitted to the rulers and did not revolt. This is key evidence for the position.
3
Understand the logical reasoning
Read the statement that infallibility is not a condition for imamate initially, so remaining is more appropriate. Reflect on why this argument supports non-removal.
This answers a potential objection that a sinful ruler should be removed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينعزل الإمام بالفسق أى بالخروج عن طاعة الله تعالى . (والجور) أى الظلم على عباد الله تعالى، لأنه قد ظهر الفسق وانتشر الجور من الأئمة والأمراء بعد الخلفاء الراشدين والسلف قد كانوا ينقادون لهم، ويقيمون الجمع والأعياد، بإذنهم، ولا يرون الخروج عليهم، ولأن العصمة ليست بشرط للإمامة ابتداء، فببقاء أولى . وعن الشافعي - رحمه الله - إن الإمام ينعزل بالفسق والجور، وكذا كل ،قاض وأمير وأصل المسألة : أن الفاسق ليس من أهل الولاية عند الشافعي - رحمه الله - لأنه لا ينظر لنفسه، فكيف ينظر لغيره ؟ . وعند أبي حنيفة - رحمه الله - هو من أهل الولاية، حتى يصح للأب الفاسق تزويج ابنته الصغيرة. والمسطور في كتب الشافعية : أن القاضى ينعزل بالفسق، بخلاف الإمام . والفرق : أن فى انعزاله ووجوب نصب غيره، إثارة الفتنة لما له من الشوكة ، بخلاف القاضي . وفى رواية النوادر عن العلماء الثلاثة : أنه لا يجوز قضاء الفاسق . وقال بعض المشايخ: إذا قلد الفاسق ابتداء يصح، ولو قلد وهو عدل ينعزل بالفسق، لأن المقلد أعتمد عدالته فلم يرض بقضائه بدونها. وفى فتاوی قاضی خان أجمعوا على أنه إذا ارتشي لا ينفذ قضاؤه فيما ارتشى وأنه إذا أخذ القاضي القضاء بالرشوة لا يصير قاضيا ولو قضى لا ينفذ قضاؤه.
The imam is not removed by sin, i.e., by going out of the obedience of Allah Almighty. And (by injustice), i.e., oppression upon the servants of Allah Almighty, because sin has appeared and injustice has spread from the imams and rulers after the Rightly Guided Caliphs, and the predecessors used to submit to them, and establish the Friday congregational prayers and the Eid prayers with their permission, and they did not see revolt against them. And because infallibility is not a condition for the imamate at its inception, so remaining is more appropriate. And it is reported from al-Shafi‘i – may Allah have mercy on him – that the imam is removed by sin and injustice, and likewise every qadi and emir. And the origin of the issue is that the sinful person is not among the people of authority according to al-Shafi‘i – may Allah have mercy on him – because he does not look after himself, so how could he look after others? And according to Abu Hanifa – may Allah have mercy on him – he is among the people of authority, so that it is valid for a sinful father to marry off his minor daughter. And it is recorded in the books of the Shafi‘is that the qadi is removed by sin, unlike the imam. And the difference is that in his removal and the obligation of appointing another, there is kindling of fitna because of his power, unlike the qadi. And in the narration of al-Nawadir from the three scholars: that it is not permissible for a sinful person to be a judge. And some shaykhs said: if a sinful person is appointed initially, it is valid; and if he was appointed while just, he is removed by sin, because the appointer relied on his justice and is not satisfied with his judgment without it. And in the Fatawa of Qadi Khan, they agreed that if he takes a bribe, his judgment concerning that bribe is not executed, and that if the judge takes the judgeship by bribery, he does not become a judge, and if he judges, his judgment is not executed.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument and comparative fiqh discussion on whether an imam (ruler) is removed from office due to sin and injustice. The author begins by stating the position: the imam is not removed by sin or injustice. He defines sin as going against Allah's commands and injustice as oppression against people. He provides historical evidence: after the Rashidun caliphs, sin and injustice appeared among rulers, yet the early generations (salaf) continued to obey them, led prayers with their permission, and did not advocate rebellion. The reason given is that infallibility (‘isma) is not a condition for assuming the imamate; if it were not required at the beginning, then it is even more appropriate not to require it later. Next, the author mentions the opposing view of Imam al-Shafi‘i, who holds that an imam is removed by sin and injustice, and the same applies to every qadi and emir. The root of the disagreement is whether a sinful person (fasiq) is qualified for authority (wilaya). For al-Shafi‘i, a fasiq is not qualified because he fails to manage his own affairs, so how can he manage others' affairs? For Abu Hanifa, a fasiq is qualified, as seen by the validity of a sinful father marrying off his minor daughter. The author then notes that in Shafi‘i books, it is stated that a qadi is removed by sin, unlike the imam. The distinction is that removing an imam and appointing another would incite fitna due to his power (shawkah), whereas removing a qadi does not. He then cites a narration from the 'three scholars' (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) that it is not permissible for a fasiq to be a judge. Some shaykhs hold that if a fasiq is appointed initially, his appointment is valid, but if he was just and then becomes sinful, he is removed because the appointer relied on his justice. Finally, from the Fatawa of Qadi Khan, there is consensus that if a judge takes a bribe, his judgment in that case is invalid, and if he obtains the judgeship through bribery, he does not become a judge and his judgments are not executed. The passage thus defends the Sunni position of not removing the imam for sin while acknowledging differences in fiqh.

Key Terms
لا ينعزل
الفسق
الجور
الإمام
العصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that if a ruler sins, he must be removed immediately.
Correction: The Sunni position, based on historical practice and logical reasoning, holds that the imam is not removed by sin because infallibility is not a condition and removing him could cause fitna.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author primarily use to argue that the imam is not removed by sin?
A) The imam is infallible.
B) The salaf did not revolt against sinful rulers after the Rashidun.
C) The Quran explicitly states this.
D) The imam's sin is private.

Key Takeaway
The imam is not removed by sin or injustice because the early community tolerated sinful rulers and infallibility is not a condition for the imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see injustice among Muslim leaders, does your heart immediately desire rebellion? How does this lesson challenge that desire, and what would it cost you to obey a sinful ruler for the sake of unity?
?
The salaf submitted to rulers even when they were oppressive. Are you willing to do the same, or do you secretly believe that your sense of justice is more reliable than the consensus of the early community?
?
If infallibility is not a condition for leadership, then what standard do you hold your leaders to? Could it be that your criticism of them stems from a hidden expectation that they should be sinless, a standard not even required by Allah?

Comparative Views on the Imam's Removal and Rulings for Judges
Author discusses differing fiqh views and rulings on judges and bribery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly views on whether a sinful ruler or judge is removed, the distinction between imam and qadi, and the rulings on bribery.

Core Idea
Scholars differ on removal of a sinful ruler, but agree that the imam is not removed due to fear of fitna.

This lesson explores the comparative fiqh discussion regarding the removal of a sinful ruler. Imam al-Shafi‘i holds that an imam is removed by sin, while Abu Hanifa disagrees. The passage also distinguishes between an imam and a judge regarding removal, and concludes with rulings on bribery and how it affects the validity of judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shafi‘i position
Start with the sentence about Imam al-Shafi‘i. He holds that the imam is removed by sin and injustice, and the same applies to any qadi or emir.
Understand the basis: the fasiq is not qualified for authority because he cannot manage himself.
2
Understand the Hanafi counter-argument
Read the Hanafi position from Abu Hanifa: a fasiq can still have authority, exemplified by a sinful father marrying off his daughter.
This shows a difference in the definition of wilaya.
3
Note the distinction between imam and qadi
Read the part from Shafi‘i books: a qadi is removed by sin, but an imam is not. The reason is that removing an imam would cause fitna due to his power (shawkah).
This distinction is key in the Shafi‘i school.
4
Review the rulings on bribery
Read the final part from Qadi Khan's Fatawa: a judge who takes a bribe does not have his judgment executed, and if he buys the judgeship, he does not become a judge.
This shows additional fiqh details related to the integrity of judges.

Praying Behind the Righteous and Wicked
Ruling on praying behind any Muslim and defense of including fiqh in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Sunni position permits praying behind any Muslim, even if sinful or innovative, and why such fiqh issues are included in a work of kalam.

Core Idea
Prayer behind any Muslim is valid, but may be disliked if the imam is sinful.

This passage addresses a practical issue of prayer leadership that also serves to mark Sunni identity against sectarian objections. The author argues from prophetic command and scholarly practice that prayer behind any Muslim is permissible, while clarifying that dislike may apply. He then defends including such a fiqh issue in a kalam work by showing it distinguishes Ahl al-Sunna from opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a ruling with evidence, a note on interpretation, an aside about the Mu'tazila, a second ruling on funeral prayer, and an objection-answer.
2
Identify the evidence for the ruling
Find the two pieces of evidence: the hadith 'Pray behind every righteous and wicked person' and the practice of the Salaf. Understand how they support permissibility.
3
Understand the objection and its answer
The objector questions why fiqh issues are in kalam. The author answers that these issues distinguish Ahl al-Sunna from opponents. Reflect on how this justifies the inclusion.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this ruling sets Sunni practice apart from other groups. Why is it important for a kalam text to address practical issues that define orthodoxy?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جواز الصلاة خلف كل بر وفاجر
وتجوز الصلاة خلف كل بر وفاجر لقوله عليه السلام. صلوا خلف كل بر وفاجر ولأن علماء الأمة كانوا يصلون خلف الفسقة، وأهل الأهواء والمبتدع من غير نكير. وما نقل عن بعض السلف من المنع عن الصلاة خلف الفاسق والمبتدع، فمحمول على الكراهة إذ لا كلام فى كراهة الصلاة خلف الفاسق والمبتدع . وهذا إذا لم يؤد الفسق أو البدعة إلى حد الكفر، وأما إذا أدى فلا كلام في عدم جواز الصلاة

ثم المعتزلة وإن جعلوا الفاسق غير مؤمن، لكنهم يجوزون الصلاة خلفه لما أن شرط الإمامة عندهم، عدم الكفر، لا وجود الإيمان، بمعنى التصديق، والإقرار والأعمال جميعا [؟] ير من اسرى

ويصلى على كل بر وفاجر إذا مات على الإيمان، للإجماع، ولقوله عليه السلام : « لا تدعو الصلاة على من مات من أهل القبلة.

فإن قيل : أمثال هذه المسائل إنما هى من فروع الفقه، فلا وجه لإيرادها في أصول الكلام، وإن أراد أن اعتقاد حقية ذلك واجب . وهذا من الأصول، فجميع مسائل الفقه كذلك.

قلنا : إنه لما فرغ من مقاصد علم الكلام من مباحث الذات والصفات والأفعال والمعاد والنبوة والإمامة على قانون أهل الإسلام وطريق أهل السنة والجماعة، حاول التنبيه على نبذ من المسائل التي يتميز بها أهل السنة من غيرهم مما خالف فيه المعتزلة أو الشيعة أو الفلاسفة أو الملاحدة، أو غيرهم من أهل البدع والأهواء. سواء كانت تلك المسائل من فروع الفقه أو غيرها من الجزئيات المتعلقة بالعقائد
Permissibility of praying behind every righteous and wicked person. And it is permissible to pray behind every righteous and wicked person because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Pray behind every righteous and wicked person.' And because the scholars of the community used to pray behind the sinful, the people of desires, and the innovator without disapproval. And what has been transmitted from some of the Salaf regarding prohibition of praying behind the sinful and the innovator is interpreted as dislike, for there is no dispute about the dislike of praying behind the sinful and the innovator. This is when the sin or innovation does not lead to the level of disbelief. But if it leads, then there is no dispute about the impermissibility of praying. Then the Mu'tazila, although they consider the sinful as not a believer, still permit praying behind him because the condition of leadership according to them is the absence of disbelief, not the existence of faith, meaning affirmation, confession, and deeds all together... And prayer is performed over every righteous and wicked person if he dies upon faith, due to consensus, and because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Do not withhold prayer over one who dies from the people of the qibla.' If it is said: Such issues are only branches of fiqh, so there is no reason to mention them in the principles of kalam, even if he intends that believing in their correctness is obligatory, and this is from the principles, then all fiqh issues are likewise. We say: When he finished the objectives of kalam regarding investigations of the Essence, attributes, actions, the return, prophecy, and leadership according to the law of the people of Islam and the way of the people of the Sunnah and community, he attempted to point out some issues by which the people of the Sunnah are distinguished from others—those in which the Mu'tazila, the Shi'a, the philosophers, the atheists, or other people of innovation and desires opposed them. Whether those issues are from the branches of fiqh or other particulars related to beliefs.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage combines a doctrinal ruling and an objection-answer. The author begins by stating the permissibility of praying behind any righteous or wicked Muslim, using the prophetic command 'Pray behind every righteous and wicked person' and the practice of the early scholars who prayed behind sinners and innovators without disapproval. Reports prohibiting this are interpreted as indicating dislike (karaha), not impermissibility, as long as the sin or innovation does not reach disbelief. The author then notes that even the Mu'tazila, who deny the faith of the grave sinner, permit prayer behind him because their condition for imamate is merely absence of disbelief. Next, the author affirms that the funeral prayer is performed over any Muslim who dies in faith, based on consensus and a hadith. An imaginary objector questions why such fiqh issues are brought into kalam. The answer explains that after covering the main topics of kalam—Essence, attributes, actions, eschatology, prophethood, imamate—the author includes selected issues that distinguish Ahl al-Sunna from opponents, whether from fiqh or other details related to beliefs. This justifies the inclusion of practical rulings when they serve to clarify Sunni distinctiveness.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position permits prayer behind any Muslim despite sin, while also showing that fiqh rulings can be part of kalam when they highlight sectarian distinctions.

Refraining from Speaking Ill of the Companions
The author commands refraining from mentioning the Companions except with goodness and explains the ruling on slandering them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on mentioning the Companions and the graded rulings for slandering them.

Core Idea
Speaking ill of the Companions is forbidden; the sin's gravity depends on the evidence it contradicts.

This passage from the ʿAqīdah al-Nasafiyyah establishes a key Sunni principle: the Companions must be spoken of only with goodness. The author supports this with three prophetic hadiths and then clarifies the ruling for those who defame them. By distinguishing between disbelief and sin, the passage provides a nuanced understanding of the seriousness of such acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle and hadiths
Read the opening sentence and the three hadiths cited. Notice how the Prophet prohibits insulting the Companions and emphasizes their superiority.
The hadiths are transmitted in authentic collections.
2
Understand the virtues of major Companions
The author mentions that there are authentic hadiths on the merits of Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman, 'Ali, al-Hasan, al-Husayn, and others. Recognize that this affirms the high status of all Companions.
3
Grasp the ruling on slandering: two categories
The passage distinguishes between slandering that contradicts decisive evidence (like slandering 'A'isha) – which is kufr – and other forms of slander, which are bid'a and fisq. Note the example given.
Decisive evidence includes clear Qur'anic verses.
4
Reflect on the distinction between kufr and bid'a/fisq
Consider why some slander amounts to disbelief while other slander is sinful but not apostasy. The author's classification protects the creed while acknowledging different severities.
This reflects the nuanced approach of Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونكف عن ذكر الصحابة، إلا بخير ) لما روى في الأحاديث الصحيحة من مناقبهم، ووجوب الكف عن الطعن فيهم، كقوله عليه الصلاة السلام : « لا تسبوا أصحابي، فلو أن أحدكم أنفق مثل أحد ذهب ما بلغ مد أحدهم ولا نصيفه وكقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: أكرموا أصحابي ، فإنهم خياركم الحديث . وكقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : «الله الله في أصحابي ، لا تتخذوهم غرضا من بعدى، فمن أحبهم فيحبى أحبهم، ومن أبغضهم فببغضى أبغضهم، ومن آذاهم فقد آذانی و من آذاني فقد آذى الله، ومن آذى الله، فيوشك أن يأخذه .

ثم في مناقب كل من أبي بكر وعمر وعثمان وعلى والحسن والحسين وغيرهم من أكابر الصحابة أحاديث صحيحة

وما وقع بينهم من المنازعات والمحاربات فله محامل وتأويلات. فسبهم والطعن فيهم إن كان مما يخالف الأدلة القطعية، فكفر. كقذف عائشة رضى الله عنها، وإلا فبدعة وفسق .
And we refrain from mentioning the Companions except with goodness, because of what has been transmitted in authentic hadiths of their merits and the obligation to refrain from defaming them, like his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Do not insult my Companions, for if one of you were to spend the equivalent of Mount Uhud in gold, he would not reach the measure of a mudd of one of them, nor half of it.' And like his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Honor my Companions, for they are the best of you.' And like his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Fear Allah concerning my Companions, do not make them a target after me. Whoever loves them loves them with my love, and whoever hates them hates them with my hatred, and whoever harms them has harmed me, and whoever harms me has harmed Allah, and whoever harms Allah, He will soon seize him.' Then regarding the merits of each of Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman, 'Ali, al-Hasan, al-Husayn, and other eminent Companions, there are authentic hadiths. And whatever disputes and wars occurred among them have interpretations and explanations. So insulting them and defaming them, if it contradicts decisive evidence—like slandering 'A'isha, may Allah be pleased with her—is disbelief. Otherwise, it is innovation and sin.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement regarding how to treat the Companions. The author states the Sunni principle: we refrain from mentioning the Companions except with goodness. This is supported by three prophetic hadiths: the first prohibiting insulting them and highlighting their superiority; the second commanding to honor them; the third warning against making them a target, with severe consequences. The author then notes that there are authentic hadiths on the virtues of the major Companions: the first four caliphs, al-Hasan, al-Husayn, and others. Regarding the conflicts that occurred among the Companions, the author says they have interpretations and explanations (tawīlāt), implying that one should not engage in criticism. Finally, the author gives a ruling on insulting and defaming them: if it contradicts decisive proofs—such as slandering 'A'isha, whose innocence is established in the Qur'an—it is disbelief (kufr). Otherwise, it is innovation (bid'a) and sin (fisq). This distinguishes between levels of transgression.

Correction: The author specifies that only slandering that contradicts decisive evidence (e.g., slandering 'A'isha) is kufr; otherwise it is bid'a and fisq (innovation and sin).

Key Takeaway
The principle of refraining from speaking ill of the Companions is supported by strong hadiths, and the ruling on defaming them varies: kufr if it contradicts decisive evidence, otherwise bidʿa and fisq.

Interpreting Conflicts Among the Companions
Classification of rulings on reviling Companions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the conflicts among the Companions have valid interpretations and that reviling them carries different rulings depending on the severity of the attack.

Core Idea
Not all criticism of Companions is the same — some is kufr, some is bid'ah.

This passage from the Aqida al-Nasafiyyah addresses a sensitive topic: the conflicts that occurred among the Companions. The author assures us that these disputes have valid interpretations, and then classifies the ruling on those who revile the Companions. As you read, notice how the author distinguishes between types of attack.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the names
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the specific Companions mentioned: Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali, al-Hasan, and al-Husayn.
The author emphasizes that authentic hadiths exist about their merits.
2
Understand the principle of interpretation
The author states that the conflicts among them have 'interpretations and explanations.' Reflect on why this is important for maintaining respect for the Companions.
This implies that their actions are not to be judged harshly.
3
Grasp the two categories of reviling
The passage divides reviling into two categories: one that contradicts definitive proofs (kufr) and one that does not (bid'ah and fisq). Identify the example given for the first category.
Slandering Aisha is given as an example of kufr because her innocence is a definitive proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم في مناقب كل من أبي بكر وعمر وعثمان وعلى والحسن والحسين وغيرهم من أكابر الصحابة أحاديث صحيحة. وما وقع بينهم من المنازعات والمحاربات فله محامل وتأويلات. فسبهم والطعن فيهم إن كان مما يخالف الأدلة القطعية، فكفر. كقذف عائشة رضى الله عنها، وإلا فبدعة وفسق.
Then regarding the merits of each of Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali, al-Hasan, al-Husayn, and others among the greatest Companions, there are authentic hadiths. And whatever occurred among them of disputes and wars has interpretations and explanations. So reviling them and attacking them—if it contradicts definitive proofs, then it is disbelief (kufr), such as slandering Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her; otherwise it is innovation (bidʿah) and transgression (fisq).
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is both an affirmation and a classification. The author first reaffirms that there are authentic hadiths regarding the merits of the major Companions, specifically naming the first four caliphs and al-Hasan and al-Husayn. Then he addresses the conflicts that occurred among them, stating that these events have plausible interpretations (maḥāmil) and explanations (taʾwīlāt). This implies that the Companions are not to be blamed for their actions, as they had valid reasons. Next, he classifies the ruling on those who revile and attack the Companions: if the attack contradicts definitive proofs (adillah qaṭʿiyyah) from the Qur'an and Sunnah—such as slandering Aisha, whose innocence was revealed in the Qur'an—then it is kufr (disbelief). If the reviling does not reach that level, it is bidʿah (innovation) and fisq (transgression).

كفر (kufr)
بدعة (bidʿah)
فسق (fisq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All criticism of Companions is equally sinful or equally permissible.
Correction: The author distinguishes: reviling that contradicts definitive proofs (like slandering Aisha) is kufr, while other reviling is bid'ah and fisq.

Checkpoint

A) It is a major sin but not disbelief.
B) It is disbelief (kufr) because it contradicts definitive proof.
C) It is innovation (bidʿah).
D) It is permissible if done with interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Conflicts among Companions have valid interpretations, and reviling them is either kufr (if contradictory to definitive proofs) or bid'ah and fisq (otherwise).

Ruling on Cursing Muawiyah and His Supporters
The author states that cursing Muawiyah and his supporters is not transmitted from the Salaf because their action was rebellion, which does not warrant a curse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Salaf did not permit cursing Muawiyah and his supporters, recognizing the distinction between rebellion and disbelief in Islamic law.

Core Idea
Rebellion does not warrant a curse; only disbelief does.

This passage addresses a sensitive historical issue. The author clarifies the position of the early scholars on cursing the participants in the conflict between Ali and Muawiyah. By examining their reasoning, we learn an important principle: not every sin, even grave ones like rebellion, justifies a curse.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the key terms: السلف, اللعن, البغى.
The passage is concise but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the reasoning
The author gives a reason: 'because the utmost of their affair was rebellion... and that does not necessitate cursing.' Reflect on why rebellion is distinguished from disbelief in this ruling.
Cursing is reserved for those who die upon kufr or are explicitly cursed in revelation.
3
Apply the principle
Consider how this principle guides our attitude towards historical figures who committed grave sins but did not die upon disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة لم ينقل عن السلف المجتهدين والعلماء الصالحين، جواز اللعن على معاوية وأعوانه لأن غاية أمرهم البغى والخروج عن طاعة الإمام الحق . وهو لا يوجب اللعن.
In summary, it has not been transmitted from the early mujtahid Salaf and the righteous scholars the permissibility of cursing Muawiyah and his supporters, because the utmost of their affair was rebellion (baghy) and departure from obedience to the rightful imam, and that does not necessitate cursing.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a concise ruling on a specific historical issue. The author states that the Salaf (the early pious generations) and the mujtahid scholars did not transmit any permission to curse Muawiyah and his supporters. The reason given is that the worst characterization of their actions is that they engaged in baghy (rebellion) and disobeyed the rightful imam (presumably Ali ibn Abi Talib). Rebellion, though a grave sin, does not by itself warrant a curse (laʿn). Cursing is reserved for those who die upon kufr or are explicitly cursed in revelation. Hence, the Salaf refrained from cursing them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rebellion automatically warrants a curse like disbelief.
Correction: Rebellion is a grave sin but does not by itself justify a curse; only disbelief or explicit cursing in revelation does.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Salaf did not curse Muawiyah because rebellion, though serious, does not warrant a curse.

?

Ruling on Cursing Yazid ibn Muawiyah
The author discusses differing opinions on cursing Yazid and argues for the curse based on mutawatir meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic legal ruling on cursing Yazid ibn Muawiyah according to the author, including the differing scholarly opinions and the basis for the author's conclusion.

Core Idea
Yazid is cursed because his satisfaction with killing al-Husayn is established by tawatur.

This passage addresses a sensitive historical figure: Yazid ibn Muawiyah. The author navigates between a general prohibition on cursing Muslims and specific circumstances that warrant exception, grounding his conclusion in the certainty of transmitted reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the initial disagreement and the author's final ruling.
The passage is dense with legal reasoning.
2
Identify the two opposing views
Find the view that forbids cursing (based on the Prophet's prohibition) and the view that allows it (due to Yazid's order to kill al-Husayn).
3
Note the agreed principle
All scholars agree that it is permissible to curse the one who killed al-Husayn, ordered it, permitted it, or was pleased with it.
This is the key to the author's argument.
4
Understand the author's use of tawatur
The author says the meaning of Yazid's satisfaction is mutawatir (massively transmitted), even if details are ahad (solitary). This establishes certainty.
Tawatur of meaning is sufficient for certainty.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that he does not hesitate about Yazid's affair—he deserves the curse. Ponder how this reconciles the earlier disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما اختلفوا في يزيد بن معاوية حتى ذكر في الخلاصة وغيرها : أنه لا ينبغى اللعن عليه ولا على الحجاج) لان النبي عليه السلام نهى عن لعن المصلين، ومن كان من أهل القبلة. وما نقل من لعن النبي عليه السلام لبعض من أهل القبلة، فلما أنه يعلم من أحوال الناس ما لا يعلمه غيره. وبعضهم أطلق اللعن عليه لما أنه كفر حين أمر بقتل الحسين رضى الله عنه . واتفقوا على جواز اللعن على من قتله أو أمر به أو أجازه أو رضى به. والحق : أن رضا يزيد بقتل الحسين واستبشاره بذلك : وإهانته أهل بيت النبي عليه السلام مما تواتر معناه وإن كانت تفاصیله آحادا فنحن لا نتوقف في شأنه، بل في إيمانه - لعنة الله عليه وعلى أنصاره وأعوانه .
And they only differed concerning Yazid ibn Muawiyah, such that it was mentioned in al-Khulāṣah and others: 'It is not appropriate to curse him nor al-Hajjaj,' because the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, forbade cursing those who pray and those who are from the people of the qiblah. And what has been transmitted of the Prophet's cursing some people of the qiblah is because he knows of people's states what others do not know. And some of them allowed cursing him because he disbelieved when he ordered the killing of al-Husayn, may Allah be pleased with him. And they agreed on the permissibility of cursing the one who killed him, or ordered it, or permitted it, or was pleased with it. And the truth is that Yazid's satisfaction with the killing of al-Husayn, his rejoicing at that, and his humiliation of the family of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, are such that their meaning is mutawatir (massively transmitted), even if the details are ahad (solitary reports). So we do not hesitate concerning his affair, rather concerning his faith. The curse of Allah be upon him and upon his supporters and helpers.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the differing scholarly opinions on cursing Yazid ibn Muawiyah and then gives the author's own position. It begins by noting that there is disagreement: some works like al-Khulāṣah state that it is not appropriate to curse Yazid or al-Hajjaj, because the Prophet forbade cursing those who pray and are from the people of the qiblah (i.e., Muslims). The Prophet's cursing of some Muslims was based on his special knowledge of their hidden states. However, others allowed cursing Yazid on the grounds that he became a disbeliever when he ordered the killing of al-Husayn. The author then notes that all scholars agreed on the permissibility of cursing the one who killed al-Husayn, or ordered it, or allowed it, or was pleased with it. From this, the author deduces that since Yazid was satisfied with the killing, rejoiced, and insulted the Prophet's family, and this is so well-established that its overall meaning is mutawatir (massively transmitted) even if the details are ahad (solitary), we do not hesitate about his state—rather we are certain that he deserves the curse. The author concludes with an explicit curse: 'The curse of Allah be upon him and upon his supporters and helpers.'

Key Terms
يزيد بن معاوية (Yazid ibn Muawiyah)
اللعن (al-laʿn)
المصلين (al-muṣallīn)
أهل القبلة (ahl al-qiblah)
تواتر (tawātur)
آحاد (āḥād)
الحسين (al-Ḥusayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the author's ultimate ruling on Yazid ibn Muawiyah?
A) It is not appropriate to curse him because he was a Muslim.
B) He is cursed because his satisfaction with killing al-Husayn is established by mutawatir meaning.
C) The matter is uncertain, so we should remain silent.
D) He is to be considered a great sinner but not cursed.

Key Takeaway
The author holds that Yazid ibn Muawiyah is cursed because his satisfaction with killing al-Husayn is established by mutawatir meaning, falling under the agreed category of those who are pleased with the killing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the principle that it is permissible to curse anyone who approves of evil, do you ever find yourself approving of sins in your heart? How can you train yourself to hate the sin without hating the sinner until the truth is established?
?
The author says the meaning of Yazid's satisfaction is certain even if details are weak. Do you have an area of your faith where you accept a general truth but doubt because of weak details? What would it take for you to submit to the mutawatir meaning?
?
If you were among the scholars who forbade cursing Muslims, would you be able to maintain that principle in the face of clear evidence of approval of evil? What does your answer show about your reliance on general rules vs. specific exceptions?

Ruling on Cursing Muawiya and Yazid
Discusses permissibility of cursing Muawiya and Yazid, and scholarly disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced rulings on cursing historical figures like Muawiya and Yazid, and why scholars differ based on the nature of their actions (disbelief vs transgression).

Core Idea
Cursing a Muslim requires disbelief, not just sin; knowledge of private faith matters.

This passage from a Hanafi creedal commentary tackles a sensitive topic: whether it is permissible to curse Muawiya and Yazid. The author navigates between early scholarly caution and later views that considered Yazid's actions as disbelief. You'll see how the principles of takfir and the limits of cursing apply to historical figures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the structure: first Muawiya, then Yazid, then the author's conclusion.
The passage includes Arabic parenthetical marks – these are for emphasis.
2
Identify the two rulings
Notice that the author treats Muawiya and his supporters differently from Yazid. For Muawiya, cursing is not permitted because rebellion does not necessitate cursing. For Yazid, there is disagreement.
The key distinction is that Muawiya's sin is transgression (bughy), while Yazid's is alleged disbelief (kufr).
3
Understand the author's final stance
The author sides with those who curse Yazid, based on the mutawatir meaning of his approval of killing al-Husayn. Reflect on how the author justifies certainty despite some details being ahad.
This illustrates how scholars use the concept of tawatur (mass transmission) in matters of historical fact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة لم ينقل عن السلف المجتهدين والعلماء الصالحين، جواز اللعن على معاوية وأعوانه لأن غاية أمرهم البغى والخروج عن طاعة الإمام الحق . وهو لا يوجب اللعن. وإنما اختلفوا في ( يزيد بن معاوية حتى ذكر في الخلاصة وغيرها : أنه لا ينبغى اللعن عليه ولا على الحجاج) لان النبي عليه السلام نهى عن لعن المصلين، ومن كان من أهل القبلة وما نقل من لعن النبي عليه السلام لبعض من أهل القبلة، فلما أنه يعلم من أحوال الناس ما لا يعلمه غيره. وبعضهم أطلق اللعن عليه لما أنه كفر حين أمر بقتل الحسين رضى الله عنه . واتفقوا على جواز اللعن على من قتله أو أمر به أو أجازه أو رضى به. والحق : أن رضا ( يزيد ) بقتل «الحسين» واستبشاره بذلك : وإهانته أهل بيت النبي عليه السلام مما تواتر معناه وإن كانت تفاصیله آحادا فنحن لا نتوقف في شأنه، بل في إيمانه - لعنة الله عليه وعلى أنصاره وأعوانه .
And in summary, nothing has been transmitted from the predecessors who were mujtahids and the righteous scholars regarding the permissibility of cursing Muawiya and his supporters, because the utmost of their affair was transgression (bughy) and departure from obedience to the rightful imam, and that does not necessitate cursing. They only differed concerning Yazid ibn Muawiya, to the extent that it was mentioned in al-Khulasa and others: that it is not appropriate to curse him nor al-Hajjaj, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade cursing those who pray, and those who are of the people of the qibla. And what has been transmitted of the Prophet's cursing some of the people of the qibla is because he knew of people's states what none other knows. And some of them pronounced cursing upon him because he disbelieved when he ordered the killing of al-Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him). And they agreed upon the permissibility of cursing the one who killed him, or ordered it, or permitted it, or was pleased with it. And the truth is that Yazid's being pleased with the killing of al-Husayn and rejoicing at that, and his insulting the household of the Prophet (peace be upon him), is something whose overall meaning is mutawatir, even if its details are ahad; so we do not hesitate regarding his affair, but regarding his faith – the curse of Allah be upon him and upon his supporters and helpers.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on cursing Muawiya and Yazid. First, the author states that the early scholars (salaf) did not permit cursing Muawiya because his actions were rebellion (bughy) against the rightful imam, which does not warrant the curse (la'n) that is reserved for disbelief. Then, regarding Yazid, the author notes a difference of opinion: some say he should not be cursed because the Prophet forbade cursing those who pray and are from the people of the qibla (i.e., Muslims), and any cursing by the Prophet was based on special knowledge. Others allowed cursing Yazid because he disbelieved by ordering the killing of al-Husayn. All agree on cursing the one who killed or approved of the killing. The author concludes that Yazid's approval of the killing and insulting the Prophet's family is established by tawatur in its overall meaning, so we are certain of his evil state, though we differ on whether he remained a Muslim. The author ends by pronouncing a curse on Yazid and his supporters.

Key Terms
لعن
معاوية
يزيد
بغي
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that all Sunnis unanimously curse Yazid without exception.
Correction: The passage shows a difference of opinion among scholars; some refrained from cursing him due to the general prohibition against cursing Muslims.

Checkpoint
Why did the early scholars not permit cursing Muawiya according to the passage?
A) Because he was a companion.
B) Because his actions were rebellion, not disbelief.
C) Because he repented before death.
D) Because the Prophet forbade cursing anyone.

Key Takeaway
Cursing is reserved for those who die on disbelief; transgression alone does not warrant it, but certainty of disbelief based on mutawatir meaning can justify it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about someone you consider sinful, do you ever find a secret pleasure in their downfall, even if only in your heart? How does Yazid's pleasure at Husayn's death—which the author condemns—mirror the hidden attitudes in your own soul? What would it take for you to root out that pleasure and replace it with grief and hope for guidance?
?
The Prophet knew the unseen states of people, but you do not. When you are tempted to curse or condemn a fellow Muslim for their wrongdoing, do you pause to consider that you might be claiming a knowledge that belongs only to Allah? How can you train your tongue to withhold judgment where certainty is lacking?
?
Yazid's faith is portrayed as uncertain—the author does not categorically declare him a disbeliever but invokes a curse based on mutawatir evil. Are you comfortable with that tension? Does it push you to leave matters of faith and final judgment to Allah, or does it give you a subtle license to condemn those you dislike? Be honest: where is your heart leaning, and what does that reveal about your own iman?

The Grave Weight of Wiping Over Khuffs
Al-Karkhi warns of kufr for denying wiping over khuffs due to tawatur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Karkhi considered rejection of wiping over khuffs as a cause for fear of disbelief and how this practice is rooted in mass-transmitted traditions.

Core Idea
Rejecting an established mutawatir sunnah can endanger one's faith.

Al-Karkhi makes a startling claim: that he fears disbelief for anyone who rejects wiping over khuffs during ablution. This is not merely a fiqhi opinion; for the early Sunnis, this practice was a clear marker of orthodoxy, backed by a body of traditions so strong that denying them bordered on rejecting the Prophet's established sunnah. Let's examine his reasoning and see why this issue carried such weight.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Karkhi's statement
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice the strong language: 'I fear disbelief for the one who does not consider wiping over the khuffs as permissible.'
This is a bold claim. Reflect on why a scholar would say this.
2
Understand the basis of tawatur
Al-Karkhi says the traditions on this topic are 'in the level of mass transmission.' This means they are so widely reported that their authenticity is certain. Rejecting such a proven sunnah is a serious matter.
Tawatur is a technical term in hadith and kalam.
3
Connect to sectarian identity
Al-Karkhi concludes that the rejecter is 'from the people of innovation.' This shows that the issue is not just legal but theological: it distinguishes Sunnis from others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الكرخى : ( إني أخاف الكفر على من لا يرى المسح على الخفين . لأن الآثار التي جاءت فيه في حيز التواتر . وبالجملة من لا يرى المسح على الخفين فهو من أهل البدعة : حتى سئل أنس بن مالك رضى الله عنه عن أهل السنة والجماعة فقال : . أن تحب الشيخين ولا تطعن في الختنين ، وتمسح على الخفين.
And al-Karkhi said: 'Indeed I fear disbelief for the one who does not consider wiping over the khuffs as permissible, because the traditions that have come concerning it are in the level of mass transmission. And in short, whoever does not consider wiping over the khuffs as permissible is from the people of innovation.' Until Anas ibn Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, was asked about Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, and he said: 'That you love the two shaykhs and do not disparage the two sons-in-law, and you wipe over the khuffs.'
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a twofold statement regarding the practice of wiping over leather socks (khuffs) in ablution. First, al-Karkhi declares that he fears disbelief for those who reject this practice, because the narrations (athar) supporting it have reached the level of tawatur (mass transmission), making their denial equivalent to rejecting an established Sunnah. He then concludes that such a person is from the people of innovation (ahl al-bid'ah). Second, the passage cites the famous response of Anas ibn Malik when asked about the defining characteristics of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah: love for the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar), not disparaging the two sons-in-law (Uthman and Ali), and wiping over the khuffs. This shows that the practice is a touchstone of Sunni identity, distinguishing them from the Rafidis (Shia) who reject it.

Key Terms
المسح على الخفين
التواتر
أهل البدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think wiping over khuffs is a minor fiqhi detail with no theological consequences.
Correction: Al-Karkhi shows it is a boundary marker of Sunni identity; denying it can be a sign of innovation and even endanger one's faith.

Checkpoint
According to al-Karkhi, what is the status of the traditions about wiping over khuffs?
A) They are of limited authenticity.
B) They are in the level of tawatur (mass transmission).
C) They are weak.
D) They are abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Wiping over khuffs is supported by mutawatir traditions, making its denial a serious matter that can lead to charges of innovation and even fear of disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there any sunnah practices that you privately downplay or dismiss because they seem culturally specific or unimportant? How does al-Karkhi’s fear of kufr - even for a ‘small’ act like wiping over khuffs - challenge you to re-examine your attitude toward the entire sunnah? This week, what is one prophetic practice you will commit to reviving, even if it feels inconvenient?
?
Al-Karkhi does not merely disagree with those who reject wiping over khuffs; he places them outside the fold of mainstream Muslims. Would you have the same courage to call out a rejection of a clear sunnah in your own environment? If not, what stops you - and what does your hesitation reveal about your conviction in the authority of the Prophet ﷺ?
?
You likely accept wiping over khuffs as a valid practice, but do you actually practice it? If not, what does your inaction say about the weight you give to a mutawatir sunnah? This passage warns that neglect can border on rejection. Will you take a concrete step today to act on this sunnah and turn this knowledge into obedience?

Anas's Three Signs of Ahl al-Sunnah
Anas defines Ahl al-Sunnah with three practical and doctrinal criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the three defining signs of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah as narrated from Anas ibn Malik.

Core Idea
Love the two shaykhs, respect the two sons-in-law, and wipe over khuffs.

When asked to define Ahl al-Sunnah, the venerable Companion Anas ibn Malik gave a concise answer that combines love for the early leaders, respect for the Prophet's family, and adherence to a specific practice. This definition shows that Sunni identity is not just abstract creed but also concrete actions and loyalties. Let's unpack his three criteria.

Guided Path
1
Read Anas's definition
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the three elements: love for the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar), not disparaging the two sons-in-law (Uthman and Ali), and wiping over khuffs.
Each element reflects a key Sunni position.
2
Identify the personalities
The 'two shaykhs' refer to the first two caliphs, Abu Bakr and Umar. The 'two sons-in-law' are Uthman and Ali, both married to daughters of the Prophet. Love and respect for all four is a hallmark of Sunnism.
This contrasts with the Rafidis who reject the first three caliphs.
3
Understand the practice
Wiping over khuffs is included as a third sign. This shows that Sunnis consider this practice an integral part of their identity, distinguishing them from those who reject it.
This connects back to the earlier assertion by al-Karkhi.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a twofold statement regarding the practice of wiping over leather socks (khuffs) in ablution.
